Five Situations After Tashahhud: The Masbuq's Prayer in Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute over the masbuq's prayer after imam's qahqaha or hadath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the effect of qahqaha and intentional hadath from taslim and speech on the masbuq's prayer, and explain the reasoning behind Abu Hanifa's and Sahibayn's positions.

Core Idea
For the masbuq, building on a spoiled part invalidates; for the imam, no building needed.

When the imam laughs loudly or intentionally invalidates his wudu after the tashahhud, the prayer of those following him may be affected, especially if they are masbuqs. In this lesson, we analyse al-Khujandi's classification of five actions after tashahhud and the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the masbuq. You will learn the exact legal reasoning of Abu Hanifa and his two disciples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Five Actions
List the five actions after tashahhud mentioned: qahqaha, intentional hadath, taslim, speech, and standing. Note which three are agreed upon as not affecting any follower's prayer.
The agreed three are taslim, speech, and standing.
2
State the Dispute
For qahqaha and intentional hadath, state that Abu Hanifa rules the masbuq's prayer as invalid (fasid), while Sahibayn rule it as valid (sahih).
The imam's own prayer and the lahiq's prayer are valid by consensus.
3
Trace Abu Hanifa's Proof
Explain that qahqaha spoils the portion of the imam's prayer that the masbuq is following. Since the masbuq must build his prayer on the imam's, building on a spoiled part makes his entire prayer invalid.
The imam does not need to build, so his prayer is unaffected.
4
Trace the Sahibayn's Proof
Explain that Sahibayn draw an analogy to taslim and speech: the muqtadi's prayer is built on the imam's in validity and invalidity. Since the imam's prayer remains valid, so does the muqtadi's.
They do not accept the distinction between needing to build and not needing.
5
Determine the Practical Consequence (Thamarah)
According to Abu Hanifa, the masbuq must repeat his prayer. According to Sahibayn, he completes it normally. This is the thamarah al-khilaf.
The text does not give tarjih; later Hanafi practice often follows Abu Hanifa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ الخُجَنْدِي: الْإِمَامُ إِذَا قَهْقَهَ بَعْدَ مَا قَعَدَ قَدْرَ التَّشَهُّدِ، أَوْ أَحْدَثَ مُتَعَمِّداً وَخَلْفَهُ لَاحِقُونَ وَمَسْبُوقُونَ، فَهٰذَا عَلَى خَمْسَةِ أَوْجُهٍ: الْقَهْقَهَةُ وَالْحَدَثُ الْعَمْدُ وَالسَّلَامُ، وَالْكَلَامُ، وَالْقِيَامُ: فَثَلَاثَةٌ مِنْهَا: صَلَاةُ الْكُلِّ تَامَّةٌ، فِي السَّلَامِ، وَالْقِيَامِ، وَالْكَلَامِ، بِالِاتِّفَاقِ. وَأَمَّا الْقَهْقَهَةُ، وَالْحَدَثُ الْعَمْدُ فَصَلَاةُ الْإِمَامِ وَمَنْ هُوَ بِمِثْلِ حَالِهِ: تَامَّةٌ. وَأَمَّا صَلَاةُ الْمَسْبُوقِينَ : فَفَاسِدَةٌ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ؛ لِأَنَّ الْقَهْقَهَةَ مُفْسِدَةٌ لِلْجُزْءِ الَّذِي تَلَاقِيَهُ مِنْ صَلَاةِ الْإِمَامِ، فَيَفْسُدُ مِثْلُهُ مِنْ صَلَاةِ الْمُقْتَدِي، غَيْرَ أَنَّ الْإِمَامَ لَا يَحْتَاجُ إِلَى الْبِنَاءِ، وَالْمَسْبُوقُ مُحْتَاجٌ إِلَيْهِ، وَالْبِنَاءُ عَلَى الْفَاسِدِ: فَاسِدٌ. بخلاف السلام؛ لأنه منه، والكلام في معناه، وينتقض وضوء الإمام لوجود القهقهة في حرمة الصلاة. وَعِنْدَهُمَا: لَا تَفْسُدُ صَلَاةُ الْمَسْبُوقِينَ؛ لِأَنَّ صَلَاةَ الْمُقْتَدِي بِنَاءٌ عَلَى صَلَاةِ الْإِمَامِ جَوَازاً وَفَسَاداً، وَلَمْ تَفْسُدْ صَلَاةُ الْإِمَامِ، فَكَذَا صَلَاتُهُمْ، فَصَارَ كَالسَّلَامِ وَالْكَلَامِ. وَلَوْ أَنَّ الْإِمَامَ قَهْقَهَ بَعْدَ مَا قَعَدَ قَدْرَ التَّشَهُّدِ، أَوْ أَحْدَثَ مُتَعَمِّداً: فَإِنَّ الْقَوْمَ يَذْهَبُونَ مِنْ غَيْرِ سَلَامٍ. وَإِنْ سَلَّمَ أَوْ تَكَلَّمَ : كَانَ عَلَيْهِمْ أَنْ يُسَلِّمُوا؛ لِأَنَّ السَّلَامَ وَالْكَلَامَ مَنْهِيَّانِ وَالْقَهْقَهَةُ وَالْحَدَثُ مُفْسِدَانِ.
Al-Khujandi said: If the imam laughs loudly after sitting the amount of tashahhud, or intentionally does hadath, and behind him are lahiqs and masbuqs, then this has five aspects: loud laughter, intentional hadath, taslim, speech, and standing. Three of them: the prayer of all is complete, in taslim, standing, and speech, by agreement. As for loud laughter and intentional hadath, the prayer of the imam and those in his same state is complete. As for the prayer of the masbuqs: it is invalid according to Abu Hanifa because loud laughter spoils the part that it encounters from the imam's prayer, so the like of it from the muqtadi's prayer is spoiled, except that the imam does not need to build, while the masbuq needs to build, and building on the invalid is invalid. Unlike taslim because it is from him, and speech is in its meaning, and the imam's wudu is nullified due to the occurrence of loud laughter in the sacred state of prayer. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): the prayer of the masbuqs is not invalid because the muqtadi's prayer is built upon the imam's prayer in permissibility and invalidity, and the imam's prayer did not become invalid, so likewise their prayers, so it becomes like taslim and speech. And if the imam laughs loudly after sitting the amount of tashahhud, or deliberately does hadath, then the congregation leave without taslim. And if he says taslim or speaks, then they must say taslim; because taslim and speech are prohibited (during prayer) while loud laughter and hadath are invalidators.
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute presented by the commentator al-Khujandi. It details five possible actions by the imam after the tashahhud: loud laughter (qahqaha), intentional hadath, taslim, speech, and standing. For three—taslim, standing, and speech—there is agreement (bil-ittifaq) that the prayers of all (imam, muqtadi, masbuq) are complete. For qahqaha and intentional hadath, there is disagreement. According to Abu Hanifa, the masbuq's prayer becomes invalid because these actions spoil the portion of the imam's prayer that the masbuq is following. Since the masbuq must build his prayer upon the imam's, building on a spoiled part is invalid. The imam's prayer is complete because he has no need to build further. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) hold that the masbuq's prayer remains valid, reasoning that the muqtadi's prayer is entirely contingent on the imam's; since the imam's prayer is not invalidated, the muqtadi's cannot be either. The passage also notes that if the imam laughs or deliberately breaks wudu, the congregation should leave without taslim. If he says taslim or speaks, they must say taslim themselves, because those actions are only prohibited (not invalidating the prayer for the masbuq).

Key Terms
القهقهة (al-qahqaha): Loud laughter in prayer, which invalidates both wudu and prayer according to Hanafis.
المسبوق (al-masbuq): One who joins the prayer after the imam has already started, missing some rak'ahs.
اللاحق (al-lahiq): One who starts with the imam but misses parts due to being late in joining, or due to sleep/forgetfulness.
الحدث العمد (al-hadath al-'amd): Intentional nullification of wudu (e.g., by passing wind or touching), done deliberately during prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why is the masbuq's prayer invalid if the imam laughs loudly after tashahhud?
A) Because loud laughter invalidates the imam's prayer, so the masbuq cannot build on an invalid prayer.
B) Because the masbuq needs to build on the imam's prayer, but the part he encounters was spoiled by laughter.
C) Because the masbuq's wudu is broken by the imam's laughter.
D) Because the imam's prayer becomes invalid for the masbuq only.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, qahqaha and intentional hadath after tashahhud invalidate the masbuq's prayer according to Abu Hanifa, but Sahibayn consider it valid; the practical difference is that the masbuq must repeat his prayer if following the former view.

Ruling on Mutayammim Seeing Water During Prayer
States rule and exceptions when mutayammim sees water during prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general Hanafi ruling when a mutayammim sees water during prayer and identify the exceptions that preserve the prayer's validity.

Core Idea
Seeing water during tayammum prayer invalidates it unless the water is inaccessible (with a stranger) or hadath preceded the prayer.

What happens when a person performing tayammum prayer suddenly sees water? The general rule is that the prayer becomes invalid because the excuse for tayammum vanishes. However, the Hanafi school recognizes important exceptions based on accessibility and prior events. This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyira explains the nuances, including an intra-Hanafi disagreement on whether one can build on the prayer after a hadath interruption.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its default
The author states the general rule: if a mutayammim sees water during prayer, the prayer becomes invalid. This is based on the principle that tayammum is only valid when water is absent.
The same applies if a reliable person informs him about water.
2
Check for the 'hadath preceded' exception
If the person had a hadath (e.g., passing wind) before starting the prayer, left the prayer to perform wudu, then found water, the prayer does not become invalid. Instead, he performs wudu and builds upon his previous prayer.
There is a disagreement: al-Nihayah allows building, al-Imla' requires restart.
3
Check for the 'water with stranger' exception
If the water is with a stranger (ajnabi) who may not give it, the prayer remains valid. After finishing, he asks for water; if given, he performs wudu and repeats the prayer; if not, his tayammum remains.
This exception also applies if the water is with one's brother or friend (accessible) – then the rule of invalidity applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : ( وَإِذَا رَأَى الْمُتَيَمِّمُ الْمَاءَ فِي صَلَاتِهِ : بَطَلَتْ صَلَاتُهُ). وَكَذَا إِذَا عَلِمَ، بِأَنْ أَخْبَرَهُ عَدْلٌ بِقُرْبِ الْمَاءِ. وَهٰذَا إِذَا لَمْ يَسْبِقْهُ الْحَدَثُ، أَمَّا إِذَا سَبَقَهُ، فَانْصَرَفَ لِيَتَوَضَّأَ، فَوَجَدَ الْمَاءَ: فَإِنَّهُ يَتَوَضَّأُ وَيَبْنِي وَلَا تَبْطُلُ صَلَاتُهُ. كَذَا فِي «النِّهَايَةِ». وَقَالَ فِي «الْإِمْلَاءِ»: يَسْتَقْبِلُ، وَلَا يَبْنِي. وَقَوْلُهُ: بَطَلَتْ هٰذَا إِذَا كَانَ الْمَاءُ مُبَاحاً، أَوْ كَانَ مَعَ أَخِيهِ أَوْ صَدِيقِهِ، أَمَّا لَوْ رَآهُ مَعَ أَجْنَبِيَّ لَا تَبْطُلُ، وَيَمْضِي عَلَى صَلَاتِهِ. فَإِذَا فَرَغَ وَطَلَبَهُ مِنْهُ، فَأَعْطَاهُ تَوَضَّأَ بِهِ، وَاسْتَأْنَفَ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يُعْطِهِ: فَهُوَ عَلَى تَيَمُّمِهِ.
His statement: (And when the mutayammim sees water during his prayer: his prayer becomes invalid). And likewise if he knows, such as if a reliable person informs him that water is near. And this is if the hadath did not precede him; however, if it preceded him, and he left to perform wudu, and then found water: then he performs wudu and builds and his prayer does not become invalid. Thus in 'al-Nihayah'. And in 'al-Imla'' it is said: He should start anew (mustaqbil) and not build. And his statement: it becomes invalid (that is) if the water is permissible (mubah), or if it is with his brother or friend. But if he sees it with a stranger (ajnabi), it does not become invalid, and he continues his prayer. Then when he finishes and asks him for it, if he gives it to him, he performs wudu with it and starts anew, and if he does not give it, he remains on his tayammum.
Source pages p. 412

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling for a person performing prayer with tayammum who sees water during the prayer. The general rule is that the prayer becomes invalid because tayammum is only permitted when water is unavailable; seeing water removes the excuse. However, there are important exceptions. First, if the person had a hadath (event that breaks wudu) before starting the prayer, and then left the prayer to perform wudu, and after that found water, he should perform wudu and build upon his previous prayer; in this case, the prayer does not invalidate. This is the view in 'al-Nihayah', but a different view from 'al-Imla'' says he must restart completely. Second, the invalidity depends on the water being accessible: if the water is with a stranger (ajnabi) who may not give it, the prayer remains valid and he continues. After finishing, if he asks and the stranger gives him water, he performs wudu and repeats the prayer; if not given, his tayammum remains valid.

Key Terms
المتيمم (al-mutayammim): A person who performs dry ablution (tayammum) due to lack of water or illness, etc.
الحدث (al-hadath): An event that invalidates ritual purity; here specifically before starting the prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) He restarts the prayer after asking the stranger.

Key Takeaway
Seeing water during tayammum prayer invalidates it only if the water is accessible; exceptions include water with a stranger or if hadath preceded and prayer was interrupted.

Ruling on the Mutayammim Seeing Water During Prayer
States when seeing water invalidates tayammum prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which seeing water invalidates a tayammum prayer and identify when the prayer remains valid according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Seeing water invalidates tayammum prayer only if the water is accessible; if inaccessible, the prayer stands.

The passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyira expands on al-Quduri's matn regarding a person performing tayammum who sees water during prayer. It clarifies that invalidation depends on the water's accessibility and the timing of the need. The commentator also draws analogies to other prayer-invalidating actions to refine the ruling and reports an intra-Hanafi difference on whether the prayer can be built upon when the need for tayammum preceded seeing water.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling and its default
The base text states: if the mutayammim sees water during prayer, his prayer is invalidated. Understand this as the default rule.
This is a ruling from al-Quduri.
2
Distinguish from other prayer-invalidating actions
The commentator compares seeing water to salam (saying salaam) and speech—these originate from the person and are prohibited, while seeing water is an external event. Yet, invalidation still occurs due to the removal of the excuse.
The distinction helps clarify the rationale.
3
Recognize the conditions for invalidation
Invalidation only occurs if the water is mubah (permissible) or with a friend/brother (accessible). If the water is with a stranger (ajnabi), the prayer is not invalidated; one continues and deals with it afterward. Also, if the event that necessitated tayammum (e.g., travel without water) preceded seeing water, the prayer is not invalidated.
Accessibility and timing are key.
4
Understand the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on building vs. restarting
When the event preceded and the mutayammim leaves to find water, al-Nihaya says he can build upon his prayer (perform wudu and continue), while al-Imla' says he must start anew. This is a legitimate difference within the school.
The relied-upon view is not given here.
5
Apply the ruling to different scenarios
Work through examples: water with friend, water with stranger, water known via trustworthy report, water found after event preceded. Determine whether prayer is invalidated or continues.
Practice solidifies the conditions.

And the ablution of the imam is invalidated due to the occurrence of loud laughter in the forbidden state of prayer. Then when he finishes and asks him for it, and he gives it to him, he performs ablution with it and begins anew; and if he does not give it to him: he remains upon his tayammum.

Commentary
This passage from the commentary of al-Jawhara al-Nayyira explains the ruling on a person performing tayammum who sees water during his prayer. The author of the matn (al-Quduri) states that seeing water invalidates the prayer. The commentator then provides several clarifications and comparisons. First, he distinguishes between seeing water and actions like salam or speech: salam and speech are acts originating from the person, so they invalidate prayer if done deliberately, but seeing water is an external occurrence. However, the ruling for seeing water is still invalidating the prayer because it indicates the availability of water, which removes the excuse for tayammum. The commentator then discusses the case of loud laughter (qahqaha) by the imam: it invalidates the imam's wudu and prayer. According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the followers' prayer is not invalidated because their prayer is based on the imam's, and the imam's prayer was not invalidated in their view? Actually the text says 'lam tafsad salat al-imam' meaning according to them the imam's prayer is not invalidated by laughter? Wait: The text says 'وعندهما: لا تفسد صلاة المسبوقين' and later 'ولم تفسد صلاة الإمام' which seems contradictory because earlier it said laughter invalidates wudu. But it likely means that in the case of the imam laughing after tashahhud, according to the Sahibayn, the imam's prayer is not invalidated because the prayer is already complete? Actually the text earlier says 'ينتقض وضوء الإمام لوجود القهقهة في حرمة الصلاة' meaning the wudu is invalidated, but the prayer may still be valid? This is a nuanced point. The commentary explains that if the imam laughs after sitting for tashahhud, the followers depart without salam because they consider the imam's prayer as ended by the laughter? Whereas if the imam says salam or speaks, the followers should return salam because those actions are prohibited but not invalidating. Then the commentator returns to the ruling on seeing water: it invalidates regardless of knowledge? Actually he specifies that it invalidates if the water is permissible (mubah) or with a friend/brother (meaning accessible), but not if with a stranger (ajnabi). In the latter case, the prayer continues, and after finishing, if he obtains the water, he must repeat the prayer; if not, his tayammum remains valid. There is also a difference of narration: in al-Nihaya, if the event (hadath) preceded the seeing, he can build upon his prayer; in al-Imla', he must start anew.

Key Terms
متيمم (mutayammim): A person who performs tayammum due to lack of water or illness.
قهقهة (qahqaha): Loud laughter with sound during prayer; it breaks wudu and invalidates prayer according to Hanafis.
سبق الحدث (sabq al-hadath): The occurrence of the event that necessitated tayammum (e.g., traveling without water) before seeing water.

Key Takeaway
Seeing water invalidates tayammum prayer only if the water is accessible; if it is with a stranger or if the need for tayammum preceded the sighting, the prayer continues with an obligation to repeat if water is later obtained.

The Twelve Issues: Exiting Prayer as Fard – Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on al-khuruj bi-sun'ihi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifa considers exiting the prayer by deliberate action (al-khuruj bi-sun'ihi) as fard, and contrast the Sahibayn's position, applying the practical consequence to the first two of the twelve issues.

Core Idea
Exiting prayer is a fard for Abu Hanifa – without it, the prayer remains incomplete.

After the final tashahhud, is the prayer over? The answer is a subtle but critical point of Hanafi law. In this lesson, we dive into the famous 'twelve issues' (al-masa'il al-ithnata 'ashariyya) concerning events that occur after tashahhud but before salam. The key is whether 'exiting by one's own action' (al-khuruj bi-sun'ihi) is a fard obligation. Abu Hanifa says yes; the two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say no. Your understanding of this dispute will determine how you rule in real cases: for example, a person making tayammum who sees water after tashahhud – is the prayer valid or not?

Guided Path
1
Identify the two issues
The passage mentions the first two of the twelve issues: (1) a mutayammim seeing water after sitting for tashahhud, and (2) a person wiping over khuffain whose wipe period expires after tashahhud. Note that both involve events that would invalidate wudu if they occurred during prayer.
These are not the only issues; the text says 'to the end', indicating a list of twelve.
2
State the underlying principle
The commentator says 'the principle in these issues is that exiting by one's own action is fard according to Abu Hanifa'. This is the root cause of the difference. For Abu Hanifa, the prayer is not fully complete until the worshipper deliberately exits (e.g., by salam or an invalidating act). For the Sahibayn, the prayer is complete with tashahhud, and salam is just a recommended exit.
This principle applies uniformly to all twelve issues.
3
Examine the Sahibayn's proof
The Sahibayn argue: if exiting were a fard act, it would have to be an act of worship like the other pillars (ruku, sujud). But exiting can be done by invalid acts like deliberate breaking of wudu or laughter, which are not acts of worship. Therefore, exiting cannot be fard.
Their argument is based on the nature of fard acts in salah – they must be acts of devotion (qurbah).
4
Examine Abu Hanifa's proofs
Abu Hanifa offers three proofs: (1) Prayer has a clear beginning (tahrim) and end (tahlil), so a deliberate act is needed to exit, just like leaving ihram in Hajj. (2) By consensus, one cannot remain in the state of ihram indefinitely after tashahhud – the prohibition shows something remains. (3) One cannot begin another prayer without exiting the current one, proving that exiting is necessary.
Abu Hanifa's first proof uses analogy (qiyas) with Hajj, which is a strong argument in Hanafi usul.
5
Derive the practical difference
The thamarah al-khilaf: For a mutayammim who sees water after tashahhud, according to Abu Hanifa the prayer is invalid (since seeing water during prayer would break tayammum), and he must repeat. According to the Sahibayn, the prayer is valid and no repetition is needed. Similarly for the expiry of wipe period.
Later Hanafi fatwa practice generally follows the Sahibayn's view (exiting not fard), so the prayer is valid in these cases.

And according to them (the Sahibayn): exiting is not fard, so the occurrence of these things is like their occurrence after salam; because if exiting were fard, it would not be performed except by an act that is a devotion, like the other pillars of bowing and prostration. And because if it were fard, it would not be performed by deliberate breaking of ablution; for it is impossible to say that the obligatory acts of prayer are performed by deliberate breaking of ablution and loud laughter. And for Abu Hanifa: that this worship has a tahrim (prohibition for entering) and a tahlil (permission for exiting), so one cannot exit from it completely except by his own action, like Hajj. And because after tashahhud, if he wanted to continue the ihram (sacred state) until the exit of the time, or the entry of the time of another prayer, he would be prevented from that by consensus; so if nothing remained of the prayer, he would not be prevented from remaining in the sitting. And because he cannot perform another prayer except by exiting from this one.
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage introduces the first two of the famous 'twelve issues' (al-masa'il al-ithnata 'ashariyya) in Hanafi fiqh, which concern what happens when certain events occur after the final tashahhud but before salam. The two issues are: (1) a mutayammim seeing water after sitting for tashahhud, and (2) a person wiping over khuffain whose period of wiping expires after tashahhud. The commentator states the underlying principle: according to Abu Hanifa, exiting the prayer by one's own action (al-khuruj bi-sun'ihi) is a fard (obligatory act of prayer). Therefore, any of these events occurring after tashahhud is like occurring during the prayer and thus invalidates it (if they are invalidators) or has the same effect as during prayer. According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), exiting by one's own action is not fard; rather, the prayer is completed by the tashahhud and salam is just a recommended exit. Hence, these events are treated like events after salam and do not invalidate the prayer. The Sahibayn provide two proofs: if exiting were fard, it would have to be an act of worship like other pillars; but exiting can be done by invalid acts like deliberate breaking of wudu or laughter, which cannot be considered pillars. Abu Hanifa responds with two arguments: first, prayer is an act of worship with a clearly defined beginning (tahrim) and end (tahlil), so one cannot fully exit except by a deliberate act, analogous to Hajj. Second, if the prayer were complete after tashahhud, one would be allowed to prolong the state of ihram indefinitely, but by consensus one is prevented from remaining in prayer indefinitely, showing that something (exiting) remains.

Key Terms
الخروج بصنعه (al-khuruj bi-sun'ihi): Exiting the prayer by one's own deliberate action (such as saying salam or intentionally breaking wudu).
التحريم والتحليل (al-tahrim wa al-tahlil): The prohibition (tahrim: entering the sacred state of prayer) and the permission (tahlil: exiting that state).
المسائل الاثنا عشرية (al-masa'il al-ithnata 'ashariyya): Twelve specific issues related to events occurring after tashahhud, based on the principle of whether exiting is fard.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The prayer is invalid and must be repeated.
B) The prayer is valid and does not need repetition.
C) The prayer is valid but he must repeat tayammum.
D) The prayer is suspended until he makes wudu.

Key Takeaway
The crux of the first two of the twelve issues is whether exiting prayer by one's own action is fard – Abu Hanifa says yes, the Sahibayn say no, and the mufta bihi is the Sahibayn's view.

Invalidators of Prayer: Nakedness, Inability, Missed Prayer, and Imam's Substitution
Listing of invalidators with brief commentary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings for four invalidators of prayer: finding a garment while naked, gaining ability to bow/prostrate, remembering a missed prayer with time for order, and substituting an ummi imam after the reciting imam's hadath.

Core Idea
Four specific events during prayer immediately invalidate it: covering 'awrah, physical ability, missed order, and imam's substitution by an ummi.

The classical Hanafi text lists sequential invalidators that end the prayer outright. The first group concerns changes in personal state or status that break the conditions of valid prayer, such as covering the 'awrah, physical capability, recollection of a missed prayer requiring order, and a flawed imam substitution. The commentary clarifies the conditions (e.g., ownership vs. permission for the garment) and the consensus that the ummi substitution invalidates.

Guided Path
1
Identify each invalidating event
From the Arabic text, list the four events: finding a garment while naked, gaining ability to bow/prostrate, remembering a missed prior prayer within its time order, and the reciting imam's hadath leading to an ummi substitute.
The text numbers these as items 5-8.
2
Determine the ruling for each event
For each event, the ruling is that the prayer becomes invalid (bāṭil) and must be restarted if time remains. Note the commentator's clarification: for the garment, 'finding' means ownership; for the missed prayer, it only invalidates if the order (tartīb) is applicable and time is ample.
The ummi substitution is said to invalidate by consensus.
3
Analyze the juridical basis
Each invalidator changes a fundamental condition (covering 'awrah, ability to perform rukū'/sujūd, sequence of obligatory prayers, or proper recitation in congregation). The text provides no explicit proofs but relies on established Hanafi rulings.
The commentary references previous ikhtilāf for the garment case (ownership vs. ibāḥa).
4
Apply to new scenarios
Consider similar cases: if a person praying with gestures due to illness unexpectedly recovers mid-prayer, or if an imam breaks wudu and appoints a non-ummi, or if the garment found is borrowed – apply the same principles.
The ummi case highlights the importance of qirā'a in imamate.

This is according to the difference of the two sayings when the shadow of everything becomes like it, and according to Abu Hanifa: twice it. His saying: (11- Or he was wiping over the splint, and it fell off due to healing). And likewise if she was a slave woman and was freed while her head was uncovered. His saying: (12- Or he was a person with an excuse, and his excuse ceased, like the mustahada and those in her meaning).
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage is a listing of twelve invalidators (nawāqiḍ) of prayer, presented as a running commentary on Mukhtasar al-Quduri. The author provides brief explanations for each. The list begins with item 5 (or possibly earlier items are on previous pages). The first ruling mentioned is for a naked person who finds a garment: the prayer is invalidated because the condition of covering ‘awrah is no longer fulfilled, and the person must start anew. The author clarifies that 'finding' here means by ownership, not by permissible use (ibāḥa), which follows a previous ikhtilāf in tayammum. The second ruling is for someone praying with gestures due to inability, but then becomes able to bow and prostrate; the prayer is invalid because the pillar of rukū‘ and sujūd must be performed. The third ruling is remembering a missed prior prayer (fā’ita): if there is time and the order (tartīb) applies, the current prayer is invalid. The fourth ruling is the imam’s ḥadath and substituting an ummi (one who cannot recite properly) — some say it invalidates by consensus because the substitute recites incorrectly. Then items 9-12: ninth, sunrise during Fajr; tenth, entry of ‘Aṣr time during Jumu‘ah (with a note on the ikhtilāf between Abu Hanifa and the two disciples about the time); eleventh, a splint falling off after healing; twelfth, cessation of a chronic excuse (like istihāḍa). The text also mentions excessive action (‘amal kathīr) in prayer — some say it invalidates, but the commentator notes a view that it does not. These rulings all cause the prayer to become invalid (bāṭil), requiring repetition.

Key Terms
سبق الحدث (sabaq al-ḥadath): The occurrence of an invalidating event during the prayer that nullifies it.
أمّي (ummī): A person who cannot recite the Qur’an properly or is illiterate.
استخلاف (istikhāf): Appointing a substitute imam after the original imam’s wudu breaks.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The current prayer is in its proper time and the missed prayer is of the same type.
C) There is ample time and the order (tartīb) applies.
D) The missed prayer is the same as the current prayer.

Key Takeaway
Four specific changes in circumstances during prayer—covering 'awrah, physical ability, remembered order, and imam's recitation—immediately invalidate the prayer and require a restart.

Invalidators of Prayer: Sunrise, Asr Time, Splint Removal, and Excuse Cessation
Second set of invalidators with commentary on timing and state changes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings for four additional invalidators: sunrise during Fajr, entry of Asr time during Jumu'ah, a splint falling off after healing, and cessation of a chronic excuse (like istihada), including the intra-Hanafi dispute on the definition of sunrise timing.

Core Idea
Time-based and physical-status changes invalidate prayer: sunrise ends Fajr, Asr time ends Jumu'ah, healed splint ends wiping, and end of excuse restores normal rules.

The second set of invalidators concerns external time markers and changes in personal status. The commentary clarifies the subtle definition of sunrise (not the disk but the rays), the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his students on the timing of Asr entry for Jumu'ah invalidation, and the implications of a splint falling off or a chronic condition ceasing. These rulings ensure prayer is performed in its proper time and state.

Guided Path
1
Understand the 'sunrise' invalidator
The text says: 'Or the sun rose during the Fajr prayer.' The commentator clarifies that this means when the rays appear such that if not for an obstacle the disk would be visible – not the actual appearance of the disk. This is the relied-upon position.
In regions with mountains, the ruling is applied when the rays are seen.
2
Analyze the 'Asr time' invalidator in Jumu'ah
If during Jumu'ah prayer the time of Asr enters, the Jumu'ah prayer becomes invalid. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa says Asr time begins when the shadow of an object is twice its height; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say when the shadow equals the object's height.
The practical consequence: depending on the ruling, the Jumu'ah may become invalid at different moments.
3
Examine the splint and excuse cessation invalidators
If a person is wiping over a splint (jabira) and it falls off due to healing, the prayer is invalid. Similarly, if a mustahada (chronic bleeding) stops, or a slave woman is freed and her head becomes uncovered, the prayer invalidates because the condition or excuse no longer exists.
These involve a change in the person's state such that the original ruling (permission to wipe, covering modifications) no longer applies.
4
Note the mention of 'excessive action'
The text includes a remark: 'Excessive action, and it is said: it does not invalidate because it is a non-invalidating action.' This indicates a disagreement about whether excessive movement during prayer invalidates.
The commentator does not resolve this ikhtilaf; it remains a point of difference.

Events Invalidating Prayer and Three Exceptions
Lists events invalidating prayer and exceptions where it becomes nafl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four events that invalidate prayer according to Abu Hanifa and distinguish the three exceptions where the prayer becomes nafl.

Core Idea
Four events invalidate prayer; three turn it to nafl if they occur during the prayer.

This passage from Al-Jawhara al-Nayyira details specific events that break the validity of the prayer according to the Hanafi school, primarily following Imam Abu Hanifa. It also highlights three exceptional cases where the prayer is not invalidated but instead transforms into a voluntary (nafl) prayer, which is crucial for the worshipper to know when to continue or restart. Additionally, an intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples regarding the timing of these events relative to the prostrations of forgetfulness is discussed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four events
From the text, list the four events that cause invalidation: sunrise during Fajr, entering Asr time during Jumu'ah, splint falling off after healing, and end of chronic excuse (e.g., mustahadah).
These are specific, not all disruptive events.
2
State the base ruling according to Abu Hanifa
Abu Hanifa holds that all four events invalidate the prayer (batil). Understand that this is the default position presented in the text.
This is the 'zahir al-riwayah' position.
3
Recognize the timing dispute after sajdat al-sahw
The text adds a nuance: if the event occurs after returning to sajdat al-sahw but before or after sitting for tashahhud, there is disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn. Note the conditions for validity according to each.
This is the key ikhtilaf in the passage.
4
Learn the three exceptions where prayer becomes nafl
The text explicitly states that the prayer does not turn into nafl except in three cases: remembering a missed fard prayer, sunrise during Fajr, and exiting of Zuhr time during Asr (or similar). Memorize these.
These are the only cases where the obligatory prayer becomes voluntary.
5
Apply the rulings to practice
Use the ruling to determine the outcome in scenarios: e.g., if sunrise occurs during Fajr, the prayer becomes nafl according to Abu Hanifa, so the worshipper may complete it as nafl and later pray Fajr again.
The mufta bihi (relied upon) position is Abu Hanifa's view.

Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage from Al-Jawhara al-Nayyira, a commentary on Al-Quduri's Mukhtasar, presents the rulings on events that occur during prayer and invalidate it, along with the intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding exceptions. The author lists four events: sunrise during Fajr, entry of Asr time during Jumu'ah, the splint falling off after healing while one is wiping over it, and the end of a chronic excuse (e.g., a mustahadah's menstruation stopping). According to Abu Hanifa, prayer is invalidated in all these cases. However, if these events occur after the worshiper has returned to the two prostrations of forgetfulness (sajdat al-sahw), then there is disagreement: if he had sat for the duration of tashahhud before the event, Abu Hanifa deems the prayer invalid, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two disciples) deem it valid; if he had not yet sat, all agree it is invalid. The text also clarifies that if the event occurs after saying salam but before the prostration of forgetfulness, the prayer is complete according to both parties—the disciples because the salam is not final until the prostration, and Abu Hanifa because salam exits the tahrimah (the state of prayer sanctity). Finally, the author states a key rule: the prayer does not turn into a supererogatory (nafl) prayer except in three cases: remembering a missed obligatory prayer during the present prayer, sunrise during Fajr, and exiting of Zuhr time during Asr (implied continuation). This is the relied-upon position of Abu Hanifa, and the text highlights the practical consequences for the worshiper.

Key Terms
سجدتا السهو (sajdatā al-sahw): Two prostrations performed at the end of prayer to compensate for forgetfulness (sahw).
التحريمة (al-tahrimah): The opening takbir (Allahu Akbar) that initiates the state of prayer (ihram).
نفلاً (naflan): Supererogatory prayer, performed voluntarily, not obligatory.
بطلت صلاته (batalat salatuhu): His prayer becomes invalid (batil).
المستحاضة (al-mustahadah): A woman experiencing chronic uterine bleeding (istihadah), not menstruation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which of the following is NOT one of the three cases where the prayer turns into nafl?
A) Remembering a missed prayer during the current prayer.
B) Sunrise during Fajr prayer.
C) Exiting of Zuhr time during Asr prayer.
D) Entry of Asr time during Jumu'ah prayer.

Key Takeaway
Prayer is invalidated by four specific events, but in three exceptional cases the prayer transforms into a voluntary (nafl) prayer—this distinction is critical for determining whether to repeat the prayer.

Hadath After Sajdatay al-Sahw: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on hadath after sajdatay al-sahw

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi intra-madhhab ruling on prayer validity when hadath occurs after sajdatay al-sahw and identify the thamarah al-khilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
The status of sajdatay al-sahw: whether it completes the prayer depends on the imam's view on tashahhud's finality.

This passage delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute triggered by the occurrence of an invalidating event (hadath) after the worshiper has returned to the two prostrations of forgetfulness (sajdatay al-sahw). The validity of the prayer hinges on two factors: whether the worshiper has sat for the duration of tashahhud and whether one follows Abu Hanifa or the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani). We will unpack the scenarios and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the trigger event
The event is the occurrence of hadath (e.g., passing wind) after the worshiper has returned from sajdatay al-sahw to the sitting position. The ruling depends on the stage of prayer at that moment.
Hadath nullifies wudu and invalidates prayer if it occurs before completing the prayer.
2
Classify the area of dispute
The passage presents three key scenarios: after sitting for tashahhud, before sitting for tashahhud, and after salam but before sajdatay al-sahw. The dispute only arises in the first scenario.
Consensus exists for the other two scenarios.
3
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa rules the prayer fasid (corrupt) if hadath occurs after sitting for tashahhud, because the prayer is not yet complete without salam. If before sitting, fasid by consensus. If after salam before sajda, the prayer is complete because salam ends the tahrimah.
Fasid means the prayer is invalid and must be repeated (or may become nafl in some interpretations).
4
State the Sahibayn's position
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) rule the prayer sahih (valid) if hadath occurs after sitting for tashahhud, as the prayer is nearly complete and the prostration of forgetfulness perfects it. They agree on the other scenarios.
The Sahibayn consider the prayer valid and no repetition is needed.
5
Extract the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if a worshiper follows Abu Hanifa, he must repeat the prayer; if he follows the Sahibayn, the prayer is accepted and no repetition is required.
This difference impacts real-life fatwa and practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحكام سبق الحدث بطلت صلاته في هذه الحالات كلها في قول أبي حنيفة . ولو عرض هذا كله بعد ما عاد إلى سجدتي السهو: فهو على هذا الخلاف. كذا في «الخُجندي». - فيحتمل أن يكون قوله: على الخلاف يعني أن عند أبي حنيفة إن كان بعد ما قعد قدر التشهد فصلاته فاسدة، وعندهما: صحيحة. وإن كان قبل قعوده قدر التشهد: فهي فاسدة، إجماعاً. ويحتمل أن تكون عندهما صحيحة. - ولو لم يقعد قدر التشهد بعد سجود السهو: فعنده فاسدة؛ لأن سجود السهو يرفع التشهد. فصلاته تامة، إجماعاً. وإن اعترض له شيء هذا بعد ما سلم قبل أن يسجد للسهو: أما عندهما فظاهر، وأما عنده ; فلأنه بالسلام يخرج من التحريمة. ولهذا لا يتغير فرض المسافر بنية الإقامة في هذه الحالة. وكذا إذا سلَّم إحدى التسليمتين؛ لأن انقطاع التحريمة يحصل بتسليمة واحدة.
Rulings of the occurrence of an invalidating event (hadath): His prayer is invalid in all these cases according to Abu Hanifa. And if all this occurs after he returns to the two prostrations of forgetfulness: then it is upon this difference of opinion. Likewise in Al-Khujandi. - It is possible that his statement: 'upon this difference of opinion' means that according to Abu Hanifa if it was after he sat for the duration of the tashahhud, then his prayer is corrupt (fasid), and according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): valid. And if it was before his sitting for the duration of the tashahhud: then it is corrupt, by consensus. And it is possible that according to the two it is valid. - And if he did not sit for the duration of tashahhud after the prostration of forgetfulness: then according to him (Abu Hanifa) it is corrupt because the prostration of forgetfulness supersedes the tashahhud. So his prayer is complete, by consensus. And if something of this occurs after he made the salutation before prostrating for forgetfulness: As for according to the two it is apparent; and as for according to him (Abu Hanifa), because by the salutation he exits from the tahrimah. And for this reason the obligation of the traveler does not change by the intention of residence in this state. Likewise if he makes one salutation, because the breaking of the tahrimah is attained by a single salutation.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of prayer when an invalidating event (hadath) occurs after the worshiper has returned to the two prostrations of forgetfulness (sajdatay al-sahw). The matn states that Abu Hanifa declares the prayer invalid in all such cases. The commentator then explains a specific scenario: if the event occurs after returning to sajdatay al-sahw, the ruling depends on whether the worshiper has sat for the duration of tashahhud. If he has sat, Abu Hanifa rules the prayer corrupt (fasid) because the prayer is still incomplete without the salam, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) rule it valid because the prayer is nearly complete. If he has not sat, it is invalid by consensus. The commentator also clarifies that if the event occurs after salam but before sajdatay al-sahw, the prayer is complete by consensus, but Abu Hanifa’s reasoning is that salam ends the tahrimah (state of consecration). The passage also notes that the traveler’s prayer does not become full (i.e., intention of residence does not affect the shortening) in this state.

Key Terms
سجدتي السهو (sajdatay al-sahw): Two prostrations performed to compensate for forgetfulness during prayer.
تشهد (tashahhud): The testimony recited while sitting in the final rak'ah.
تحريمة (tahrimah): The state of being in prayer, initiated by takbir and concluded by salam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if a hadath occurs after the worshiper returns to sajdatay al-sahw but before sitting for tashahhud, what is the ruling?
A) Valid according to all Hanafis.
B) Invalid by consensus.
C) Invalid according to Abu Hanifa but valid according to Sahibayn.
D) Valid because sajdatay al-sahw have been performed.

Key Takeaway
The validity of prayer after sajdatay al-sahw when hadath occurs depends on whether the worshiper has sat for tashahhud, with Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn differing on the completion of prayer.

The Three Exceptions: When Prayer Becomes Nafl
Explains the three cases where prayer becomes nafl instead of invalid according to Abu Hanifa, and the Sahibayn's view

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three exceptions where Abu Hanifa rules that an invalidated obligatory prayer becomes nafl, and explain the Sahibayn's counter-argument based on the hadith 'your prayer is complete'.

Core Idea
Three specific emergencies turn your invalid obligatory prayer into nafl — not wasted, but not the original obligation.

Abu Hanifa generally rules that a prayer becomes invalid when a hadath occurs. But he makes three exceptions where the prayer does not become invalid: it turns into a voluntary (nafl) prayer. The two Sahibayn disagree, holding that the prayer is completely valid. This lesson walks through the dispute, its evidence, and its practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three exceptional cases
List the three scenarios where Abu Hanifa says the prayer becomes nafl instead of invalid: (1) remembering a missed obligatory prayer during the prayer, (2) sunrise during Fajr, (3) exit of Zuhr time during Jumu'ah.
These are the only three cases; all other invalidators cause absolute invalidity.
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling and its logic
Abu Hanifa: the prayer is no longer valid as an obligation but becomes nafl. The logic: one cannot fulfill a new obligation (e.g., praying the missed prayer, Fajr on time, Jumu'ah) without exiting the current prayer. Exiting becomes obligatory, but the prayer already performed is not wasted—it counts as nafl.
Abu Hanifa uses the principle: 'Whatever is necessary to fulfill an obligation becomes obligatory itself.'
3
State the Sahibayn's opposing position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: the prayer is complete (tammah) and valid in all these cases. No need for repetition or conversion to nafl.
They rely on the hadith: 'When you say this or do this, your prayer is complete.'
4
Analyze the interpretation of the hadith
The Sahibayn take 'tammat' literally. Abu Hanifa interprets it figuratively as 'near completion' (qaraba al-tamam), similar to the hadith about Hajj: 'Whoever stands at 'Arafah, his Hajj is complete.' That does not mean literal completion; it means the essential part is done.
This is a classic usul al-fiqh issue: haqiqah (literal) vs. majaz (figurative).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
If a hadath occurs in one of these three cases: According to Abu Hanifa, the prayer is not valid as an obligation but counts as nafl; the worshiper must still make up the missed obligatory prayer later. According to the Sahibayn, the prayer is fully valid and no makeup is needed.
The difference affects whether the obligation is discharged or not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بطلت صلاته في هذه الحالات كلها عند أبي حنيفة). ولا تنقلب نفلاً، إلا في ثلاث مسائل، وهي: ١ - إذا تذكر فائتة. ۲ـ أو طلعت الشمس. ۳ـ أو خرج وقت الظهر في الجمعة، وفيما عدا هذه لا تنقلب نفلاً. المسائل قوله: (وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد : تمت صلاته في هذه كلها؛ لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: إذا قلت هذا، أو فعلت هذا: فقد تمت صلاتك. (١) قلنا : معناه : قاربت التمام، كما قال عليه الصلاة والسلام: «من وقف بعرفة فقد تم حجه (۲)، أي: قارب التمام. وله : أنه لا يمكنه أداء صلاة أخرى إلا بالخروج من هذه الصلاة، وما لا يتوصل إلى الفرض إلا به: يكون فرضاً.
His statement: (His prayer is invalid in all these cases according to Abu Hanifa). And it does not turn into nafl except in three cases: 1 - when he remembers a missed (obligatory) prayer. 2 - or the sun rises. 3 - or the time of Zuhr exits during Jumu'ah. And apart from these, it does not turn into nafl. The issues. His statement: (And Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: His prayer is complete in all these; due to his statement, peace be upon him: 'When you say this, or do this, then your prayer is complete.') We said: Its meaning is: it neared completion, just as he said, peace be upon him: 'Whoever stands at 'Arafah, his Hajj is complete.' i.e., it neared completion. And for him (Abu Hanifa): it is not possible for him to perform another prayer except by exiting this prayer, and that without which the obligatory cannot be attained becomes obligatory.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage presents the second part of the matn from Al-Quduri regarding the ruling when an invalidating event occurs during prayer. The general rule according to Abu Hanifa is that the prayer is invalid. However, there are three exceptions where the prayer does not become invalid but instead turns into a voluntary (nafl) prayer: (1) when the worshiper remembers a missed obligatory prayer during the current prayer, (2) when the sun rises during a Fajr prayer, and (3) when the time of Zuhr exits during a Jumu'ah prayer. In all other cases, the prayer becomes nafl. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ, holding that the prayer is complete (tammah) in all these cases, citing the hadith that the prayer is complete upon saying or doing the final actions. The commentator explains that the hadith means the prayer is near completion, not literally complete. The commentator adds that one cannot perform another obligation without exiting this prayer, and whatever is necessary to fulfill an obligation becomes obligatory itself.

Key Terms
نفلاً (naflan): Supererogatory prayer; the invalid obligatory prayer is counted as voluntary.
فائتة (fā'itah): A missed obligatory prayer that has not been performed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) If the worshiper sneezes.
B) If the sun rises during Fajr.
C) If the imam forgets a surah.
D) If a woman passes in front of the worshiper.

Key Takeaway
In three specific emergencies, Abu Hanifa rules that the invalid obligatory prayer becomes nafl, while the Sahibayn hold it is complete, a difference rooted in interpreting 'tammat' literally or figuratively.

Definition and Ruling of Qada' (Making Up Missed Prayers)
Definition of qada' and ruling on missed prayers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define qada' as the delivery of an equivalent of the obligatory act and state that making up missed prayers is wajib whether missed intentionally or unintentionally.

Core Idea
Qada' is wajib for any missed prayer—even deliberate omission requires making up.

In this passage, the commentator explains the distinction between ada' (performance on time) and qada' (making up after the time). He then clarifies that the obligation to make up missed prayers applies to all missed prayers, regardless of the reason. This principle is essential for understanding the duty to compensate for missed obligatory acts.

Guided Path
1
Define ada' and qada'
In your own words, state the definitions from the passage. Ada' is delivering the obligatory act itself; qada' is delivering its equivalent when unable to do the original.
The commentator uses the analogy of financial guarantees to explain qada'.
2
Note the linguistic choice: 'fawa'it' vs 'matrukat'
Why did the author use 'fawa'it'? This implies the prayers were missed due to forgetfulness, sleep, etc., not deliberate abandonment, which is the default assumption about a Muslim.
This choice reflects good opinion and avoids accusation of sin.
3
Extract the ruling from the matn
The matn says: 'Whoever misses a prayer, makes it up when he remembers it.' No exception is mentioned.
4
Extend to deliberate omission
The commentator explicitly extends the ruling: even if one deliberately leaves a prayer out of transgression or carelessness, making up is obligatory.
Thus, qada' is wajib for any missed prayer, intentional or not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب قضاء الفوائت. لما فرغ من بيان أحكام الأداء، وما يتعلق به، وهو الأصل، شرع في القضاء، وهو خَلَفه، إذ الأداء: عبارة عن تسليم نفس الواجب، والقضاء: عبارة عن تسليم مثل الواجب، والتسليم بمثل الواجب إنما يكون عند العجز عن تسليم نفسه، كما في المضمونات من حقوق العباد. والأداء يجوز بلفظ القضاء، إجماعاً. وفي القضاء بلفظ الأداء: خلاف، والصحيح أنه يجوز. وإنما قال: قضاء الفوائت، ولم يقل: قضاء المتروكات؛ لأن الظاهر من حال المسلم أنه لا يترك الصلاة عمداً، بل تفوته، باعتبار غفلة، أو نوم، أو نسيان. وإنما ذكر الفوائت بلفظ الجمع، وقال في الحج باب القوات: بلفظ الواحد؛ لأن الحج لا يجب في العمر إلا مرة واحدة. قال رحمه الله: (ومن فاتته صلاة: قضاها إذا ذَكَرَها). وكذا إذا تركها عمداً فسقاً، أو مجانةً، أي قلة مبالاة: يجب القضاء.
Chapter on Making Up Missed Prayers. When he finished explaining the rulings of performance (ada'), and what pertains to it, and it is the original, he began on making up (qada'), and it is its substitute. Indeed, performance (ada') is the delivery of the obligatory act itself, and making up (qada') is the delivery of its equivalent. The delivery of an equivalent only occurs when one is unable to deliver the act itself, as in guaranteed rights among the rights of people. Performance is permissible with the wording of qada', by consensus. Regarding qada' with the wording of ada', there is disagreement, and the correct view is that it is permissible. He only said 'qada' al-fawa'it' (making up of missed actions) and did not say 'qada' al-matrukat' (making up of abandoned actions) because the apparent state of a Muslim is that he does not leave prayer deliberately, but rather it is missed due to heedlessness, sleep, or forgetfulness. He mentioned 'fawa'it' in the plural form, and in Hajj he said 'bab al-qawat' in the singular form because Hajj is only obligatory once in a lifetime. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'Whoever misses a prayer, makes it up when he remembers it.' Likewise, if he leaves it deliberately out of transgression or carelessness, i.e., lack of concern, making up is obligatory.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on making up missed prayers (qada' al-fawa'it). The commentator first defines key terms: 'ada'' is performing the obligatory act itself at its prescribed time, while 'qada'' is performing its equivalent after the time has passed due to inability. This is analogous to financial guarantees. He notes that 'ada'' can be expressed using the word 'qada'' by consensus, but the reverse is disputed, with the correct view being permissible. The commentator explains why the author chose the term 'fawa'it' (missed things) over 'matrukat' (abandoned things): Muslims are assumed not to deliberately abandon prayers; they miss them due to forgetfulness or sleep. The plural 'fawa'it' is used for prayers because they are repeatedly obligated, unlike Hajj which is a single obligation, hence the singular in the Hajj chapter. The core ruling, expressed in the matn, is that whoever misses a prayer must make it up upon remembering it. The commentator extends this to deliberate omission: even if one leaves prayer intentionally out of sin or carelessness, making up is obligatory. This emphasizes that qada' applies regardless of the reason for missing.

Key Terms
الأداء (al-adā'): Performing an obligatory act within its prescribed time; delivering the act itself.
القضاء (al-qaḍā'): Performing the equivalent of an obligatory act after its time due to inability to perform it in time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is not required because it was done intentionally.
B) It is recommended but not obligatory.
C) It is obligatory to make it up.
D) It is invalid and cannot be made up.

Key Takeaway
Qada' is wajib for every missed prayer, whether due to forgetfulness, sleep, or deliberate carelessness.

Making Up Missed Prayers: Definition, Obligation, and Order
Defines qada', states obligation of makeup, and discusses priority rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qada' (makeup of missed prayers), explain the tartib (order) condition, and apply the three exceptions that override it.

Core Idea
Whoever misses a prayer must make it up when remembered, and it takes precedence over the current prayer unless time is tight.

Prayer is the pillar of faith, and missing it – whether due to sleep, forgetfulness, or even intentional neglect – carries a duty of make-up (qada'). The Hanafi school not only obliges qada' but also establishes a strict order (tartib) that the missed prayer must be performed before the current obligatory prayer. However, this order is not absolute; it falls away when the current prayer would otherwise be lost, when one forgets, or when the missed prayers are so many that the order becomes impractical. This lesson unpacks these rulings from the classical Hanafi text Al-Hidayah.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish ada' and qada'
Identify that ada' is performing the obligation in its time, while qada' is performing its like after the time has passed. The author uses 'fawa'it' (missed) rather than 'matrukat' (abandoned) out of good assumption about a Muslim.
This distinction is foundational for understanding when makeup becomes mandatory.
2
State the obligation of qada'
The core ruling: whoever misses a prayer – by sleep, forgetfulness, or even intentionally – must make it up when he remembers. The evidence is the hadith 'Whoever sleeps through a prayer or forgets it, let him pray it when he remembers, for that is its time.'
The imperative (falyusalliha) indicates obligation (wujub).
3
Explain the tartib (order) rule
The missed prayer must be performed before the current time prayer. This is a binding condition (shart mustahaqq) in the Hanafi school. The proof: the hadith sets the makeup's time as the moment of remembrance, so performing the current prayer first would be before its established time.
This is where Hanafi differs from Shafi'i (new view).
4
List the three exceptions that drop the tartib
The condition of tartib is waived in three cases: (1) when performing the makeup first would cause the loss of the current prayer (tight time), (2) when one forgets the order, (3) when missed prayers are so many they fall into the realm of repetition (more than five, per later detail).
In tight time, one prays the current prayer first, then makes up the missed one.
5
Analyze the invalidity when tartib is violated in ample time
If one deliberately prays the current prayer first in ample time, the current prayer is invalid (fasid) because the prohibition is specific to it and not to supererogatory prayers. The prohibition implies invalidity.
This is deduced from the hadith and the usul principle: 'Al-nahy yaqtadi al-fasad'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب قضاء الفوائت
لمَّا فَرَغَ من بيان أحكام الأداء، وما يتعلق به، وهو الأصل، شرع في القضاء، وهو خَلَفه، إذ الأداء: عبارة عن تسليم نفس الواجب، والقضاء: عبارة عن تسليم مثل الواجب، والتسليم بمثل الواجب إنما يكون عند العجز عن تسليم نفسه، كما في المضمونات من حقوق العباد.
والأداء يجوز بلفظ القضاء، إجماعاً.
وفي القضاء بلفظ الأداء: خلاف، والصحيح أنه يجوز.
وإنما قال: قضاء الفوائت، ولم يقل : قضاء المتروكات؛ لأن الظاهر من حال المسلم أنه لا يترك الصلاة عمداً، بل تفوته، باعتبار غفلة، أو نوم، أو نسيان.
وإنما ذكر الفوائت بلفظ الجمع، وقال في الحج باب القوات: بلفظ الواحد ؛ لأن الحج لا يجب في العمر إلا مرة واحدة. قال رحمه الله: (ومن فاتته صلاة: قضاها إذا ذَكَرَها).
وكذا إذا تركها عمداً فسقاً، أو مجانةً، أي قلة مبالاة: يجب القضاء أيضاً، لكن للمسلم عقلاً وديناً لا يَرِدُ عليه التفويت قصداً، فعبر عنه بالتفويت؛ لحسن ظنه به، وحملاً لأمره على الصلاح.
قوله: (وقدمها لزوماً على صلاة الوقت، إلا أن يَخافَ فَوْتَ صلاة الوقت : فيُقدم صلاة الوقت على صلاة الفائتة، ثم يقضيها).
الترتيب بين الفوائت وفرض الوقت عندنا: شرط مستحق، ويُسقطه ثلاثة أشياء : ضيق الوقت والنسيان، ودخولُ الفوائت في حيز التكرار. قوله : إلا أن يخاف فوت صلاة الوقت فيقدم صلاة الوقت على الفائتة : فلو قدم الفائتة : جاز ؛ لأن النهي عن تقديمها لمعنى في غير المنهي عنه، وهو صَوْنُ الوقتية عن الفوات.
بخلاف ما إذا كان في الوقت سعة، وقدم الوقتية: حيث لا يجوز؛ لأنه أداها قبل وقتها الثابت لها بالحديث وهو قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: «من نام عن صلاة أو نسيها فليصلها إذا ذَكَرَها، فإن ذلك وقتها فيه» (١).
ولأن النهي عن صلاة الوقتية إذا كان الوقت يتسع لهما : لمعنى يختص بها، ألا ترى أنه لو تنفل في ذلك الحال : لم يُنْهَ عنه ، وإنما يُنهى عن صلاة الوقت خاصةً، والنهي إذا اختص بالمنهي عنه: اقتضى الفساد.
(۱) صحيح البخاري (٥٩٧) ، صحيح مسلم (٣١٤، ٦٨٤).
Chapter on Making Up Missed Prayers
When he finished explaining the rulings of performance (ada') and what pertains to it, which is the original, he began with qada', which is its substitute. For ada' is the delivery of the very obligation itself, while qada' is the delivery of the like of the obligation. The delivery of the like of the obligation only occurs when unable to deliver the obligation itself, as in guaranteed rights among the rights of people.
It is permissible to use the word 'qada'' for ada', by consensus. Regarding using the word 'ada'' for qada', there is a difference of opinion, and the correct view is that it is permissible.
He said 'qada' al-fawa'it' (making up the missed ones) and not 'qada' al-matrukat' (making up the abandoned) because the apparent state of a Muslim is that he does not intentionally abandon prayer; rather it escapes him due to heedlessness, sleep, or forgetfulness.
He mentioned fawa'it in the plural, while in the Hajj chapter he said 'bab al-qawat' in the singular, because Hajj is only obligatory once in a lifetime.
He said (may Allah have mercy on him): "Whoever misses a prayer must perform it when he remembers it."
Similarly, if one intentionally leaves it out of disobedience or disregard (i.e., lack of concern), make-up is also obligatory. However, for a Muslim, rationally and religiously, intentional missing is not expected; therefore he expressed it as 'missing' due to his good opinion of him and assuming his affair to be righteous.
His statement: "And it must be given precedence over the time prayer, unless one fears losing the time prayer, then he gives precedence to the time prayer over the missed prayer, then makes it up."
The order between missed prayers and the obligatory prayer of the time, according to us, is a valid condition (shart mustahaqq), and three things remove it: limited time, forgetfulness, and that the missed prayers fall into the realm of repetition.
His statement: "Unless one fears losing the time prayer, then he gives precedence to the time prayer over the missed prayer," so if he gives precedence to the missed prayer, it is permissible because the prohibition of giving it precedence is for a reason other than the act itself, which is preserving the time prayer from being lost.
Contrary to when there is ample time and he gives precedence to the time prayer: it is not permissible because he performed it before its time established by the hadith, which is his saying (peace be upon him): "Whoever sleeps through a prayer or forgets it, let him pray it when he remembers, for that is its time." (1)
And because the prohibition of the time prayer when there is ample time for both is due to a reason specific to it. Do you not see that if one performs a supererogatory prayer in that situation, he is not prohibited; rather, only the time prayer is prohibited, and when the prohibition is specific to the prohibited act, it implies invalidity.
(1) Sahih al-Bukhari (597), Sahih Muslim (314, 684).
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on making up missed prayers (qada' al-fawa'it) in Al-Hidayah, providing the definition and key rulings. The author first distinguishes between ada' (performing an obligation in its prescribed time) and qada' (performing it after its time has passed). Ada' is the original; qada' is its substitute, like paying a debt. The rule is that qada' is obligatory for any missed prayer, whether missed due to forgetfulness, sleep, or even intentional neglect (though the author charitably assumes a Muslim would not deliberately miss prayer). The core ruling: whoever misses a prayer must make it up when he remembers. This includes both unintentional and intentional omissions, but the wording 'fawa'it' (missed) is used out of good assumption.

A critical point is the order (tartib) between missed prayers and the current time prayer. According to the Hanafi school, it is a binding condition (shart mustahaqq) that makeup prayers be performed before the current obligatory prayer. However, this condition is waived in three situations: (1) if the current prayer's time is so short that performing the makeup first would cause the current prayer to be missed; (2) if one forgets the order; (3) if the missed prayers are so many (more than five, according to later detail) that the order becomes impossible. If one fears losing the current prayer time, he should pray the current prayer first, then make up the missed one. But if time is ample, he must perform the makeup first; deliberately performing the current prayer first would be invalid (fasid) because it is performed before its proper time as established by the hadith: 'When he remembers, that is its time.' The hadith indicates that the makeup prayer's time is whenever one remembers, so the time prayer cannot be given precedence over it unless it too would be lost. The prohibition is specific to the time prayer when performed after a missed prayer in ample time, and such a prohibition renders the act invalid.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada'): Performing an obligation after its designated time has passed, by delivering its like.
فوائت (fawa'it): Plural of fa'itah, meaning prayers that have been missed (due to any cause). The author uses this word instead of 'matrukat' (abandoned) out of good assumption that a Muslim does not deliberately abandon prayers.
ترتيب (tartib): The order of precedence: performing missed prayers before the current obligatory prayer unless an exception applies.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if someone remembers a missed prayer while there is ample time left for the current obligatory prayer?
A) He must pray the current prayer first, then make up the missed one.
B) He must make up the missed prayer first; if he prays the current prayer first it is invalid.
C) He may choose either order; both are valid.
D) He should pray a supererogatory prayer instead.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school obligates making up missed prayers immediately and gives them precedence over the current time prayer, except when the current prayer would be lost, one forgets, or the missed prayers are too many.

intermediate
10 min
Order Between Missed and Current Prayers
Explaining the ruling on prioritizing missed prayers with proofs and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the order between missed and current prayers, identify the three exceptions that drop the order, and apply the ruling to scenarios involving narrow time, forgetfulness, and multiplicity of missed prayers.

Core Idea
A missed prayer must be made up immediately before the current time prayer, except when time is too narrow, one forgets, or there are many missed prayers.

The Hanafi school places great emphasis on making up missed prayers (qada') promptly. But what happens when a missed prayer is remembered at the time of a current obligatory prayer? Must the missed be prayed first, or can the current prayer go ahead? This lesson unpacks the rule and its exceptions, grounding them in the primary hadith evidence and demonstrating how the school resolves apparent conflicts between analogy and juristic preference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling on order
Read the matn statement: missed prayers are given precedence 'necessarily' over the time prayer. Note that this is a required condition (shart mustahaqq), not an absolute fard. It is a wajib that can be dropped under specific circumstances.
The phrase 'luzuman' indicates obligation, but the exceptions show it is not unconditional.
2
Recognize the three exceptions
The commentator lists three things that nullify the order: narrowness of time (diq al-waqt), forgetfulness (nisyan), and the missed prayers becoming numerous (dukhul al-fawa'it fi hayyiz al-tikrar). Memorize these three as key to applying the rule correctly.
Multiplicity generally means more than five missed prayers, but details are in longer texts.
3
Analyze the hadith proof
The hadith 'Whoever sleeps through a prayer or forgets it, let him pray it when he remembers it, for that is its time' is cited. Understand how the Hanafi school deduces that the missed prayer's time is the moment of remembrance, thus it must precede any other prayer at that moment.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but its meaning is considered definitive (qat'i al-dalala) for this ruling.
4
Distinguish qiyas from istihsan in narrow time
In a scenario where 'Asr is started in narrow time and the sun sets before completion, qiyas would invalidate 'Asr, but istihsan allows it to be completed. Understand why the Hanafi school prefers istihsan here: to avoid hardship and because the missed prayer's time is also pressing.
This istihsan overrides the strict analogy, showing the school's pragmatic approach.
5
Apply to concrete scenarios
The text gives several scenarios from al-Khujandi. Work through each: (1) beginning 'Asr unaware of missed Zuhr, (2) beginning 'Asr in narrow time, (3) remembering missed Zuhr after reddening of the sun, (4) beginning 'Asr while knowingly remembering missed Zuhr. Identify the ruling for each and which exception applies.
These scenarios illustrate how the general rule and exceptions play out in practice.

Source Text

Chapter of Making up Missed Prayers. And he (the author) said: 'They (missed prayers) are given precedence necessarily over the time prayer, except if one fears missing the time prayer, then he gives precedence to the time prayer over the missed prayer, then he makes it up.' Also, but for the Muslim intellectually and religiously, deliberate missing does not occur, so he expressed it as missing (tafwit) due to good opinion of him and interpreting his matter as upright. His statement: 'And they are given precedence necessarily over the time prayer, except if he fears missing the time prayer: then he gives precedence to the time prayer over the missed, then he makes it up.' The order between missed prayers and the obligatory time prayer according to us is a condition that is required, and it is nullified by three things: narrowness of time, forgetfulness, and the missed prayers entering the realm of multiplicity. His statement: 'except if he fears missing the time prayer, then he gives precedence to the time prayer over the missed': so if he gives precedence to the missed prayer, it is permissible, because the prohibition of giving precedence to it is for a reason outside the prohibited thing itself, which is preserving the time prayer from being missed. Unlike when there is ample time, and he gives precedence to the time prayer: it is not permissible, because he performed it before its time established by the hadith, which is the Prophet's statement, peace be upon him: 'Whoever sleeps through a prayer or forgets it, let him pray it when he remembers it, for that is its time.' And because the prohibition of the time prayer when time accommodates both is for a reason specific to it; do you not see that if he performed a supererogatory prayer at that time, he would not be prohibited, but he is only prohibited from performing the time prayer specifically, and the prohibition, when it is specific to the prohibited thing, implies invalidity. (1) Sahih al-Bukhari (597), Sahih Muslim (314, 684). As for in the case of narrow time, the prohibition of giving precedence to the missed prayer is not specific to it; rather, it is prevented only so that it does not lead to delaying the time prayer, as evidenced by the fact that if he performed a supererogatory prayer or any action, he would be prohibited for that reason. And the prohibition, when it is not for a reason in the prohibited thing itself, does not imply invalidity. Rather, the preferable in the case of narrow time is to give precedence to the time prayer, because if he starts with the missed, the time prayer will be missed, making both missed. But if he starts with the time prayer, one of them is missed. So praying one of them on time is better than both being missed. Al-Khujandi said: If he begins 'Asr at the beginning of its time, not knowing that he owes Zuhr, and prolongs the standing and recitation until the time of dislike enters, then remembers that he owes Zuhr, he may continue his prayer. And if he begins 'Asr in a state of narrow time, and after praying one or two rak'ahs the sun sets: the analogy (qiyas) is that the 'Asr is invalid, and istihsan is that he continues it, then makes up Zuhr, then prays Maghrib. And if he remembers that he owes Zuhr after the sun has reddened, he prays 'Asr, and if he prays Zuhr, it is not valid. And if he begins 'Asr at the beginning of its time while remembering that he owes Zuhr, and prolongs the standing and recitation until the disliked time enters, his prayer is not valid, and he must cut the 'Asr, then begin 'Asr a second time, then pray Zuhr after sunset.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage from the commentary (sharh) on al-Quduri's Mukhtasar discusses the ruling on the order between missed prayers (fawa'it) and the current obligatory prayer. The author begins by stating the general rule from the matn: missed prayers must be given precedence necessarily over the time prayer, unless one fears that the time prayer will be missed, in which case the time prayer is offered first, then the missed prayer is made up. The commentator explains that this order is a condition (shart) that is required (mustahaqq) but can be waived by three things: narrowness of time (diq al-waqt), forgetfulness (nisyan), and the missed prayers becoming numerous (dukhul al-fawa'it fi hayyiz al-tikrar), meaning when the number of missed prayers exceeds the number of time prayers (generally more than five, but details are in longer works).

When time is ample, if one deliberately offers the time prayer before the missed, it is invalid because the hadith establishes that the time for a missed prayer is when it is remembered, so offering a prayer before its appointed time is invalid. The commentator cites the hadith: 'Whoever sleeps through a prayer or forgets it, let him pray it when he remembers it, for that is its time.' This hadith, from Bukhari and Muslim, provides the proof. The prohibition of the time prayer when time is ample is specific to it; supererogatory prayers at that time are not prohibited. Since the prohibition is specific, it implies the invalidity of the time prayer if offered before the missed.

In the case of narrow time (e.g., only enough time for one prayer before sunset), the prohibition of offering the missed prayer first is not specific to the missed prayer itself but is to prevent missing the time prayer. One would also be prohibited from performing supererogatory prayers or any other action during that narrow time. Since the prohibition is not inherent to the missed prayer, offering it does not cause invalidity, but it is still disliked and not preferable. The preferable (al-awla) is to offer the time prayer first so that at least one prayer is performed on time.

The commentator then quotes al-Khujandi with several scenarios: (1) If one begins 'Asr at the beginning of its time unaware of a missed Zuhr, and prolongs until the disliked time, then remembers, he may continue his 'Asr (it is valid). (2) If he begins 'Asr in narrow time and the sun sets after one or two rak'ahs, the qiyas (analogy) would hold the 'Asr invalid due to missing the time, but istihsan (juristic preference) allows him to continue the 'Asr, then make up Zuhr, then pray Maghrib. This is a typical Hanafi application of istihsan over qiyas to avoid hardship. (3) If he remembers a missed Zuhr after the sun has reddened (i.e., close to sunset), he should pray 'Asr first because 'Asr time is extremely tight; praying Zuhr at that point would not be valid because the time for 'Asr is even narrower. (4) If he begins 'Asr at the beginning of its time while consciously remembering that he owes Zuhr, and then prolongs until the disliked time, his 'Asr becomes invalid because he deliberately delayed the missed prayer without excuse. He must cut the 'Asr, restart it (with proper intention to pray 'Asr after completing the missed?), then pray Zuhr after sunset. The commentator clarifies these applications from the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
قضاء الفوائت (qada' al-fawa'it): Making up missed obligatory prayers that were not performed at their prescribed times.
ترتيب (tartib): The order between missed prayers and the current time prayer; a condition that the missed prayer be offered before the time prayer.
ضيق الوقت (diq al-waqt): Narrowness of time such that only enough time remains for one prayer (the current time prayer) before its expiry.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if someone remembers a missed Zuhr prayer when there is ample time for both Zuhr and 'Asr, and he intentionally prays 'Asr first?
A) The 'Asr prayer is valid because the order is not obligatory.
B) The 'Asr prayer is invalid because the time for the missed prayer takes precedence.
C) The 'Asr prayer is valid but makruh.
D) The missed Zuhr is waived and not required.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the order between missed and current prayers is wajib and must be observed unless the time is narrow, one forgets, or the missed prayers are numerous.
