Preference for Current Prayer over Missed in Constrained Time
States the preferability of current prayer over missed in constrained time

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the order of prayers in constrained time and explain the rational proof that the prohibition is external and does not invalidate the missed prayer.

Core Idea
In constrained time, prioritize the current prayer so only one is lost; the prohibition is external, not inherent.

This passage tackles a practical scenario: you have a missed prayer to make up, but the time for the current prayer is just enough for one. Which should you start with? The Hanafi school uses rational proof (istihsan) to determine that it is better to begin with the current prayer, and that the prohibition against starting with the missed one does not make it invalid. Let's walk through the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is the order of praying when time is constrained. The ruling is that it is preferable (الأولى) to start with the current prayer rather than the missed one.
This is a sunnah ghayr muakkadah level – not a strict obligation, but strongly recommended.
2
Locate the dalil
The text provides a rational proof: the prohibition against missing the current prayer is not specific to the missed prayer; it applies to any action (like supererogatory prayer) that would delay the current prayer. This shows the prohibition is external.
The evidence is the analogy to supererogatory acts – they too are prohibited in constrained time.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The prohibition does not arise from an inherent defect in the missed prayer itself (لَمْ يَقْتَضِ الْفَسَادَ). Instead, it is a precautionary measure to avoid losing the current prayer. Hence, performing the missed prayer in constrained time is not invalid, merely discouraged.
This principle: 'a prohibition due to an external reason does not imply corruption' is key.
4
Apply the preference reasoning
The preference is based on minimizing loss: if you start with the missed prayer, both become missed. If you start with the current, only one is missed. Therefore, praying one as current is better than both as missed.
This is a rational calculation (istihsan) – preferring the lesser harm.
5
Evaluate the ikhtilaf
The Hanafi position is consistent with Zahir al-Riwayah. Other schools may differ, e.g., by requiring the missed prayer first or allowing free choice. This distinction matters for fatwa.
The Hanafi mufta bihi is to pray the current prayer first in constrained time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في حال ضيق الوقت فالنهي عن تقديم الفائتة لا يختص بها، وإنما منع منها؛ كي لا يؤدي إلى تأخير الوقتية، بدليل أنه لو تنفل، أو عمل عملاً من الأعمال : نُهي عنه ; لأجل ذلك، والنهي إذا لم يكن لمعنى في نفس المنهي عنه: لم يقتض الفساد. وإنما كان الأولى في حال ضيق الوقت : أن يقدم الوقتية; لأنه لو بدأ بالفائتة: فاتته الوقتية، فيصيران جميعاً ,فائتين فإذا بدأ بالوقتية: كانت إحداهما فائتة، فلأن يصلي إحداهما وقتيةً : أولى من أن يصليهما فائنتين.
And as for in the case of constrained time, the prohibition of advancing the missed prayer is not specific to it; rather it is prohibited from it so that it does not lead to delaying the current time prayer, evidenced by the fact that if he performed a supererogatory prayer or did any action, he would be prohibited from it for that reason. And if the prohibition is not due to an inherent meaning in the prohibited thing itself, it does not necessitate corruption. And the preferability in case of constrained time is to advance the current time prayer, because if he begins with the missed prayer, the current time prayer will be missed, and thus both become missed. So if he begins with the current time prayer, only one of them becomes missed. So it is better to pray one of them as a current prayer than to pray both as missed prayers.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This is a ruling with rational proof. The passage discusses the situation when the remaining time for the current prayer (e.g., Asr) is so short that if one starts a missed prayer, the current prayer will be missed. The Hanafi school rules that the prohibition against starting with the missed prayer is not because the missed prayer is inherently defective, but to prevent the loss of the current prayer. This is analogous to prohibiting supererogatory prayers or other acts in such time. Since the prohibition is for an external reason, it does not make the missed prayer invalid if performed. However, the preferred action (الأولى) is to pray the current prayer first, because then only one prayer becomes missed instead of both. This ruling is based on istihsan and the maxim that a prohibition for an external reason does not imply the prohibited act is corrupt.

Key Terms
فائتة (fā'itah): A prayer that was not performed in its prescribed time; a missed prayer to be made up.
وقت ضيق (waqt ḍayyiq): Time window just sufficient to complete the prayer before its expiry.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference used to derive the ruling that current prayer takes precedence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the missed prayer is inherently invalid.
B) To prevent both prayers from becoming missed.
C) Because the current prayer is optional.
D) Because the missed prayer cannot be offered after its time.

Key Takeaway
In constrained time, the Hanafi school rules that it is preferable to offer the current prayer first, as this ensures only one prayer is missed, not both.

Continuing Asr when Remembering Missed Prayer during Disliked Time
Ruling from Khujandi on continuing Asr when missed prayer is remembered after entering disliked time

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for continuing a prayer when a missed prayer is remembered during a disliked time under the condition of initial ignorance.

Core Idea
When you start a prayer unaware of a missed obligation, you may continue it even if it extends into a disliked time.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from Al-Khujandi regarding the validity of continuing an Asr prayer when one remembers a missed Zuhr after entering the time of karahah. Understanding the conditions of applicability helps clarify when an obligation does and does not interrupt an ongoing act.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and context
The person begins the Asr prayer at its earliest time, unaware that he owes a Zuhr prayer.
Unawareness at the start is crucial.
2
Identify the obstacle
He prolongs his qiyam and qira'ah such that the prayer extends into the time of karahah (disliked time).
The disliked time here is makruh tanzih, not tahrimi.
3
Identify the new information
He remembers the missed Zuhr prayer while still in the Asr prayer.
The memory occurs after entering disliked time.
4
Apply the ruling
The ruling is that he may continue his Asr prayer; it remains valid.
The prayer is not invalidated by the remembered obligation due to initial ignorance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الخجندي : إذا افتتح العصر في أول الوقت وهو لا يعلم أن عليه الظهر، وأطال القيام والقراءة حتى دخل وقت الكراهة، ثم تذكر أن عليه الظهر فله أن يمضي على صلاته.
Al-Khujandi said: If he begins Asr at the beginning of its time while not knowing that he owes Zuhr, and he prolongs the standing and recitation until the disliked time enters, then he remembers that he owes Zuhr, then he may continue his prayer.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This is a specific ruling attributed to the scholar Al-Khujandi. It addresses a scenario where a person starts praying Asr at its earliest time, unaware of an outstanding Zuhr prayer that is still owed. He lengthens his qiyam and qira'ah so much that the prayer extends into the time of karahah (disliked time, e.g., when the sun begins to turn yellow). At that point he remembers his missed Zuhr. The ruling is that he may continue his Asr prayer; it remains valid. The key factors are his ignorance at the start and the fact that he did not intentionally delay. This ruling is based on the Hanafi principle that if the prohibition is not due to the act itself, remembering the missed prayer after entering disliked time does not invalidate the current prayer.

Key Terms
وقت الكراهة (waqt al-karāhah): Time when it is disliked to pray, such as when the sun begins to turn yellow until it sets.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He must stop and pray Zuhr immediately.
B) He may continue his Asr prayer.
C) He must repeat Asr after making up Zuhr.
D) He cannot continue and his Asr is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling permits continuation of a prayer entered in ignorance of a missed obligation, even if it extends into a disliked time.

Istihsan Over Qiyas When Asr Extends Past Sunset
Compares qiyas and istihsan rulings when Asr extends past sunset in constrained time

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of istihsan to determine the ruling for a prayer that extends beyond its prescribed time due to unavoidable circumstances.

Core Idea
When Asr starts in constrained time and sunset occurs mid-prayer, istihsan overrides qiyas to preserve validity.

In Hanafi fiqh, the relationship between qiyas (analogy) and istihsan (juristic preference) is a hallmark of the school. This passage presents a classic case: a person begins Asr when only its own time remains, and the sun sets before completion. By strict qiyas, the prayer would be invalid, but the Hanafi school applies istihsan to rule it valid, allowing the worshipper to complete the prayer. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and its practical outcome.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Understand the case: a person starts Asr in constrained time (only enough for Asr) and the sun sets after one or two rak'ahs.
The prayer is begun in good faith.
2
Understand the qiyas argument
By qiyas, because the prayer extended beyond its time, it should be invalid (fasid). The original case is any prayer outside its time.
Qiyas is a valid tool but can be overridden.
3
Understand the istihsan argument
Istihsan overrides qiyas here to preserve validity, based on ease (taysir) and the worshipper's good intention.
The school prefers istihsan in this case.
4
Note the practical consequence
The person completes Asr, then makes up any missed Zuhr, then prays Maghrib.
This is the mufta bihi ruling.
5
Reflect on the broader principle
Istihsan is a distinctive Hanafi source that allows flexibility when strict analogy would cause hardship.
It demonstrates the school's emphasis on ease.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن افتتح العصر في حال ضيق الوقت، فلما صلى منها ركعة أو ركعتين غربت الشمس : فالقياس أن تفسد العصر، والاستحسان أن يمضي فيها، ثم يقضي الظهر، ثم يصلي المغرب.
And if he begins Asr in a state of constrained time, and when he has prayed one or two rak'ahs from it the sun sets: then the qiyas (analogy) is that the Asr becomes invalid, and the istihsan (juristic preference) is that he continues in it, then makes up Zuhr, then prays Maghrib.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage presents a conflict between qiyas and istihsan. By qiyas, if someone starts Asr when time is tight (i.e., the remaining time is just enough for Asr), and the sun sets before completing it, the prayer should become invalid because it was performed outside its time. However, the Hanafi school applies istihsan: the prayer remains valid, and he should complete it. Afterwards, he must make up the missed Zuhr (if he owes it) and then pray Maghrib. This istihsan is based on the principle that the one who begins a prayer in good faith should not be penalized for an unavoidable delay, and that the obligation of the current prayer takes precedence over strict analogical reasoning. This ruling is mufta bihi.

Key Terms
قياس (qiyās): Analogical reasoning that would invalidate the Asr because it extended past its time.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference that deems the Asr valid despite sunset.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where Asr begins in constrained time and the sun sets after one or two rak'ahs, what is the mufta bihi Hanafi ruling?
A) The Asr is invalid and must be repeated.
B) He continues the Asr, then makes up Zuhr, then prays Maghrib.
C) He stops Asr and prays Maghrib immediately.
D) He must repeat Asr after Maghrib.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, istihsan can override qiyas to preserve the validity of a prayer begun in good faith, even if sunset occurs before completion.

Prioritizing Asr When Remembering Missed Prayer After Sun Turns Red
Ruling to pray Asr when remembering missed Zuhr after sun turns red

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a missed Zuhr is remembered after the sun has turned red and explain why Asr takes precedence.

Core Idea
Asr takes priority over a missed Zuhr when the sun turns red; praying Zuhr at that time is invalid.

When the sun turns red, Asr time is nearly over. If you remember a missed Zuhr at that moment, you must not delay the current Asr. This lesson explains that ruling and why the current obligation prevails.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the time condition
Identify that the sun has turned red (iḥmarat al-shams). This is the critical condition that triggers the ruling.
The time after the sun turns red is considered makruh for prayer but the obligation remains.
2
Apply the rule
The ruling is: pray the current Asr prayer, not the missed Zuhr. This is the correct action.
The passage explicitly states 'fa innahu yuṣallī al-ʿaṣr'.
3
Understand the rationale
Asr time is extremely tight; praying Zuhr would cause Asr to be missed entirely. The current obligation takes precedence.
Hanafi fiqh prioritizes preserving the current obligatory prayer.
4
Identify the consequence of the opposite action
If one prays Zuhr instead, that Zuhr is invalid (lā yajūz). It does not fulfill the missed obligation.
The consequence reinforces the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if he remembers that he owes Zuhr after the sun has turned red: then he prays Asr, and if he prays Zuhr, it is not valid.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This is a clear ruling: if someone remembers an outstanding Zuhr prayer after the sun has turned red (i.e., the time for Asr is extremely tight), he must pray the current Asr prayer. If he mistakenly prays the missed Zuhr instead, that Zuhr prayer is invalid (لا يجوز). The reason is that the time for Asr is now so short that if he prays Zuhr, Asr will be missed entirely. Since Asr is the current obligation, it takes precedence. This ruling applies strictly when the sun has turned red, which is a sign of the end of Asr time.

Key Terms
احمرت الشمس (iḥmarat al-shams): The sun turned red, indicating the final part of Asr time.

Key Takeaway
When the sun turns red, the current Asr prayer takes precedence over any missed prayer; praying the missed prayer at that time is invalid.

Asr Started Knowing Missed Prayer and Prolonged into Disliked Time
Ruling on Asr when started with knowledge and prolonged into disliked time

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for Asr prayer when started with knowledge of a missed prayer and prolonged into the disliked time, and explain the required remedial actions.

Core Idea
Asr started knowingly missing Zuhr and prolonged into makruh time is invalid; cut, restart, then make up Zuhr after sunset.

In this passage from Al-Jawhara al-Nayyira, we encounter a specific scenario where a person begins Asr prayer at its earliest time, fully aware that they owe a missed Zuhr prayer, and then prolongs the prayer into the disliked time (when the sun turns yellow). The ruling is clear: the Asr prayer becomes invalid and must be abandoned, restarted quickly to finish before sunset, and the missed Zuhr must be offered after sunset. This lesson sharpens your ability to apply rulings regarding the order of prayers and the impact of disliked times.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The person starts Asr at its beginning time, but is mindful (dhākir) of a missed Zuhr prayer.
Knowledge of the missed prayer is key; ignorance would change the ruling.
2
Recognize the prolongation into disliked time
He intentionally prolongs the standing and recitation so that the prayer enters the disliked time (waqt makrūh).
The disliked time here is when the sun turns yellow, just before sunset.
3
State the ruling
The Asr prayer is not valid (lā tajūz). It is batil (void) and must be cut off.
Validity is negated because of the combination of knowledge and delay into disliked time.
4
Outline the required actions
He must cut the Asr prayer, then begin Asr anew (presumably quickly to avoid further delay), and after sunset he must pray the missed Zuhr.
The missed Zuhr is made up after Asr (after sunset) because Asr time expires at sunset.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This ruling contrasts with the earlier one from Al-Khujandi. The key difference is that here the person knows he owes a missed Zuhr from the start. Despite knowing this, he begins Asr and prolongs it into the makruh time (e.g., when the sun turns yellow). In this case, his Asr prayer is invalid (لا تجوز). He must break off that prayer, then restart Asr (presumably quickly to finish before sunset), and after sunset he makes up the missed Zuhr. The ruling is based on the fact that he knowingly delayed while having an obligation to make up the missed prayer first, and his delay into disliked time shows negligence.

Key Terms
ذاكر (dhākir): Being aware/remembering that he owes a missed prayer.
وقت مكروه (waqt makrūh): Disliked time for prayer, e.g., when the sun turns yellow.

Key Takeaway
Starting a prayer while knowing of a missed obligation and prolonging it into a disliked time invalidates the prayer; it must be cut and restarted, and the missed prayer made up after the current prayer's time ends.

Obligation of Tartib in Making Up Missed Prayers
States the obligation to make up missed prayers in order and provides dalil from hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on tartib in making up missed prayers and cite the hadith of Khandaq as evidence.

Core Idea
Missed prayers must be made up in order; the Prophet's action at Khandaq proves it is wajib.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling on the order (tartib) when making up multiple missed prayers. The passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayyira clearly states the obligation and provides the key evidence from the Prophet's practice during the Battle of the Trench. Understanding this ruling is essential for anyone performing qada' correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Read the opening sentence: 'If several prayers are missed by him, he should make them up in order as they were originally obligated.' The Hanafi ruling is that tartib is wajib (obligatory).
The term 'ربَّبها' (arranged in order) directly indicates the requirement.
2
Locate the dalil
The passage cites the hadith that the Prophet was occupied on the day of Khandaq and missed four prayers (Zuhr, Asr, Maghrib, Isha). He made them up in order later that night.
The hadith is recorded in Sunan al-Tirmidhi (179), Sunan al-Nasa'i, and Sahih al-Bukhari (631).
3
Understand the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's action of making up the prayers in order, combined with his general command 'Pray as you have seen me praying', indicates that order is a binding part of the prayer. The Hanafi school uses this to establish wujub.
The author notes that the Prophet did not say 'pray as I pray' because no one can match his khushu', but the command covers the external actions, including order.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if several prayers are missed by him, he should make them up in order as they were originally obligated. And the evidence for the obligation of order is that the Prophet, peace be upon him, was occupied on the day of Khandaq from four prayers, and he made them up in order. Then he said: 'Pray as you have seen me praying,' and this is a command for order. And he did not say: 'Pray as I pray' or 'as I prayed,' because it is not within anyone's capacity to pray as he prayed in humility. The four prayers from which he was occupied on the day of Khandaq were Zuhr, Asr, Maghrib, and Isha. He made them up after a period of the night, that is a portion of the night, about a third or a quarter of it. He commanded Bilal to give the adhan, then the iqamah, and he prayed Zuhr, then iqamah for Asr, then iqamah for Maghrib, then iqamah for Isha. Commentary
This passage establishes the obligation of tartib (order) when making up multiple missed prayers. The ruling is that missed prayers must be made up in the sequence they were missed. The proof is the hadith of the Prophet on the day of the Battles of the Trench (Khandaq). He was so occupied that he missed four prayers: Zuhr, Asr, Maghrib, and Isha. Later that night, he made them up in that order. He also said, 'Pray as you have seen me praying,' which is a general command to follow his manner, including order. The author notes that the Prophet did not say 'as I pray' (present tense) or 'as I prayed' (past tense) because no one can match his level of khushu' (humility), but the command applies to the actions he performed, such as ordering. The hadith is quoted with references to Tirmidhi, Nasa'i, and Bukhari. This ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
ترتيب (tartīb): The obligation to make up missed prayers in the order they were missed.
قضاء (qaḍā'): Making up missed prayers after their time has passed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Logical analogy

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that missed prayers must be made up in order (tartib) as wajib, proven by the Prophet's action at Khandaq and his general command to follow his prayer.

Ordering Missed Prayers (Tarteeb) in Qada: Obligation, Proof, and Exception
Ruling with proof and exception for tarteeb in qada

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on tarteeb in qada, identify the hadith proof, and apply the exception when missed prayers exceed six.

Core Idea
Order in qada is wajib up to six missed prayers; beyond that, order drops.

When you miss multiple prayers, should you make them up in order? The Hanafi school holds that order (tarteeb) is obligatory, but only up to six missed prayers. This lesson examines the ruling, its proof from the Prophet's practice during the Battle of the Trench, and the exception where tarteeb is no longer required.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is making up missed prayers (qada). The author states that one must arrange them in order (tarteeb) corresponding to the original obligation sequence.
The original obligation sequence refers to the chronological order of the missed prayers.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) on the Day of the Trench where he missed four prayers and made them up in order, along with his general command 'Pray as you have seen me praying.'
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's action demonstrates the practice, and the command (amr) indicates wujub according to Hanafi usul. However, since the hadith is ahad, the obligation is wajib, not fard.
Hanafi usul classifies commands from ahad hadith as wajib, not fard.
4
Note the exception
The text states that if missed prayers exceed six, order is dropped. This is because maintaining order becomes too burdensome.
The exception applies when the missed prayers become six and the time for the seventh enters.
5
Evaluate the exception's application
The commentator clarifies that the exception is triggered when the missed prayers have reached six and the seventh prayer's time has entered, meaning order for all is no longer required.
The wording 'exceed six' includes the scenario where one has six and a seventh becomes missed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب قضاء الفوائت
وإن فاتته صلوات : ربَّبها في القضاء كما وجبت في الأصل،
ولو افتتح العصر في أول الوقت وهو لا يعلم أن عليه الظهر، وأطالها حتى دخل وقت الكراهة، ثم تذكر أن عليه الظهر : فله أن يمضي على صلاته.
* قوله : (فإن فاتته صلوات ربَّبها في القضاء كما وجبت في الأصل). أي عند قلة الفوائت بدليل قوله فيما بعد إلا أن تزيد الفوائتُ على ست صلوات.
:
والدليل على وجوب الترتيب: «أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم شغل يوم الخندق عن أربع صلوات، فقضاهن مرتباً (۱).
ثم قال: «صلوا كما رأيتموني أصلي»، وهذا أمر بالترتيب. وإنما لم يقل: صلوا كما أصلي أو كما صليت؛ لأنه ليس في وسع أحد أن يصلي كما صلى في الخشوع.
والأربع الصلوات التي شغل عنها يوم الخندق الظهر والعصر والمغرب والعشاء، فقضاهنَّ بعد هَوِي من الليل، أي طائفة من الليل، وهي نحو من ثلثه الليل، أو ربعه، فأمر بلالاً فأذن، ثم أقام فصلى الظهر، ثم أقام فصلى العصر ، ثم أقام فصلى المغرب، ثم أقام فصلى العشاء.
إلا أن تزيد الفوائتُ على ست صلوات : فيسقط الترتيب فيها .
قوله: (إلا أن تزيد الفوائتُ على ستّ صلوات: فيسقط الترتيب فيها).
مراده: أن تصير الفوائت ستاً، ودخل وقت السابعة: فإنه يجوز أداء السابعة.
وفيه إشكال، وهو: أن بدخول السابعة لا تزيد الفوائت على ست، وإنما ذلك بخروج وقت السابعة.
والجواب: أن هذا من باب إطلاق اسم الأغلب على الكل، فإن الأغلب أن خروج السادسة لا يكون إلا بدخول السابعة، وعند دخول السابعة : يتحقق فواتُ الست والسابعة بعرضية أن تفوت.
وقيل : معناه: إلا أن تصير الفوائتُ ستاً، وتحمل الزيادة على الست بالوتر.
Chapter on Making Up Missed Prayers. And if he missed prayers, he should arrange them in order when making them up as they were obligated originally. And if he began Asr at its early time while he does not know that he owes Dhuhr, and he lengthened it until the time of dislike entered, then he remembered that he owes Dhuhr: he may continue his prayer. His saying: 'If he missed prayers, he should arrange them in order when making them up as they were obligated originally' means when the missed prayers are few, as indicated by his later statement 'unless the missed prayers exceed six prayers'. The evidence for the obligation of order is that the Prophet (peace be upon him) was occupied on the Day of the Trench from four prayers, so he made them up in order. Then he said: 'Pray as you have seen me praying,' and this is a command for order. He did not say 'pray as I pray' or 'as I prayed' because no one can pray as he prayed in humility. The four prayers he was occupied from on the Day of the Trench are Dhuhr, Asr, Maghrib, and Isha. He made them up after a portion of the night, meaning a part of the night, about one-third or one-quarter of the night. He ordered Bilal to give the adhan, then iqama, and he prayed Dhuhr, then iqama and prayed Asr, then iqama and prayed Maghrib, then iqama and prayed Isha. Unless the missed prayers exceed six prayers: then order is dropped in them. His saying: 'Unless the missed prayers exceed six prayers: then order is dropped in them.' His intent: that the missed prayers become six, and the time of the seventh enters: then it is permissible to perform the seventh. And there is an issue in this, which is that with the entry of the seventh, the missed prayers do not exceed six; that only happens with the exit of the time of the seventh. The answer: this is from the category of applying the name of the more common to the whole, for the more common is that the exit of the sixth does not occur except with the entry of the seventh, and upon entry of the seventh, the loss of the six and the seventh is realized by the contingency that it might be lost. And it is said: its meaning is unless the missed prayers become six, and the increase beyond six is carried over by the witr.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage from Al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah explains the obligation of tarteeb (order) when making up missed prayers. The author begins by stating the ruling: missed prayers must be made up in the same order they were obligated, implying the order of the day. He then cites the incident of the Prophet (peace be upon him) during the Battle of the Trench, where he missed four prayers and made them up in order. The command 'Pray as you have seen me pray' is understood as an order for tarteeb. The passage also clarifies that the hadith mentions the Prophet making up four prayers, and they were Dhuhr, Asr, Maghrib, and Isha. Then the exception is introduced: if the missed prayers exceed six, the obligation of order is dropped. The commentator discusses an interpretational difficulty regarding the number six and the entrance of the seventh prayer, resolving it by explaining that the text uses a common expression where the entrance of the seventh prayer is a sign that the sixth has exited. There is also an alternative interpretation that the exception applies when the missed prayers become six and an additional prayer (witr) is considered.

Key Terms
ترتيب (tarteeb): The order in which missed prayers are made up, matching the order they were originally obligated.
فوائت (fawa'it): Missed obligatory prayers that must be made up (qada).

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) When missed prayers exceed six
C) When missed prayers are exactly six
D) When missed prayers are fewer than six

Key Takeaway
Tarteeb in qada is wajib for up to six missed prayers; beyond that, order is not required.

Loud Recitation in Congregational Qada
Ruling on recitation in qada: loud in congregation, choice when alone.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on loud recitation in congregational qada and explain the preference when praying alone.

Core Idea
In qada congregation, the imam must recite loudly in jahr prayers; when alone, loud is better.

This lesson covers the rules for recitation when making up missed prayers. Whether in congregation or alone, the same principles of jahr (loud) and sirr (silent) apply as in the original prayer time, with slight variation in obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
The act under discussion is making up missed prayers (qada).
2
Distinguish two scenarios
The rule differs depending on whether the qada is performed in congregation or alone.
Congregation means at least two people, with an imam.
3
Ruling for congregation
If the missed prayer is from those normally recited loudly (Maghrib, Isha, Fajr), the imam must recite loudly.
This is wajib on the imam.
4
Ruling for praying alone
If praying alone, the person has a choice between loud and silent recitation, but loud is better (afdal).
Choice is permissible, but following the original time's recommendation is superior.
5
Note the basis
The rulings are based on the analogy (qiyas) with the original prayer times: 'ka-ma fi al-waqt' (as in the original time).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومتى قضى الفوائت : إن قضاها بجماعة، وكانت يُجهر فيها: جهر الإمام فيها بالقراءة، وإن قضاها وحده يتخيَّر، والجهر أفضل، كما في الوقت.
ولو قضى بعض الفوائت حتى قل ما بقي: عاد الترتيب عند البعض، وهو الأظهر.
وقال بعضهم : لا يعود، وهو اختيار أبي حفص؛ لأن الساقط لا يتصور عوده.
And when he makes up missed prayers: if he makes them up in congregation, and among them are those in which recitation is loud, the imam recites loudly in them; and if he makes them up alone, he may choose, but loud recitation is better, as in the original time. And if he makes up some of the missed prayers until what remains is little, tarteeb returns according to some, and it is the more apparent view. And some of them say: it does not return, and that is the choice of Abu Hafs; because that which has dropped cannot be imagined to return.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage continues with additional rulings on qada. First, if one makes up missed prayers in congregation, the imam should recite loudly if those prayers are normally recited loudly (i.e., Maghrib, Isha, and Fajr). If praying alone, one has a choice between loud and silent, but loud is better, just as in the original time. Then, the passage addresses the question of whether tarteeb returns if one makes up some missed prayers until only a few remain. The stronger position (al-azhar) is that tarteeb does return. However, some scholars, including Abu Hafs, hold that it does not return because once the order is dropped, it cannot be revived. The text indicates the first view is more apparent, making it the relied-upon opinion.

Key Terms
جهر (jahr): Loud recitation of the Qur'an in prayers that are normally recited loudly (Maghrib, Isha, Fajr).
فوائت (fawa'it): Missed prayers that are being made up (qada).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He must recite loudly if it is a jahr prayer.
B) He may choose between loud and silent, but loud is better.
C) He must recite silently.
D) He must follow the imam's ruling even if alone.

Key Takeaway
The rules for recitation in qada mirror those of the original time, with a concession when alone: loud is recommended but not obligatory.

6 min
Return of Tarteeb When Few Missed Prayers Remain
Intra-Hanafi dispute on return of tarteeb when most missed prayers are made up.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute on the return of tarteeb when few missed prayers remain and identify the preferred view.

Core Idea
When only a few missed prayers remain, tarteeb (order) returns—this is the more apparent Hanafi view.

This lesson addresses a nuanced question within the Hanafi school: once the obligation to make up missed prayers in order is dropped due to many missed prayers, does it return when most have been made up? The text presents two views and favors the return of tarteeb.

Guided Path
1
Understand the rule of tarteeb
The obligation to make up missed prayers in order (tarteeb) applies when the number of missed prayers is five or fewer. When it exceeds five (or six in some views), the order drops.
The exact threshold is discussed elsewhere; here the focus is on its potential return.
2
Identify the question
If a person had more than five missed prayers and makes up most of them until only a few remain, does the obligation of tarteeb come back?
'Few' is typically defined as three or fewer (some say five).
3
State the two positions
Some Hanafi scholars say tarteeb returns ('al-azhar' view). Others, including Abu Hafs, say it does not return because that which has dropped cannot be imagined to return (الساقط لا يتصور عوده).
4
Analyze the opposing proof
Abu Hafs' reasoning: the obligation of tarteeb was dropped entirely due to the large number; once dropped, it is as if it never existed, so it cannot be revived.
This is a principle in Hanafi usul: al-saqit la ya'ud (the dropped does not return).
5
Determine the tarjih
The text declares the first view 'al-azhar' (more apparent), making it the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position in the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومتى قضى الفوائت : إن قضاها بجماعة، وكانت يُجهر فيها: جهر الإمام فيها بالقراءة، وإن قضاها وحده يتخيَّر، والجهر أفضل، كما في الوقت.
ولو قضى بعض الفوائت حتى قل ما بقي: عاد الترتيب عند البعض، وهو الأظهر.
وقال بعضهم : لا يعود، وهو اختيار أبي حفص؛ لأن الساقط لا يتصور عوده.
And when he makes up missed prayers: if he makes them up in congregation, and among them are those in which recitation is loud, the imam recites loudly in them; and if he makes them up alone, he may choose, but loud recitation is better, as in the original time. And if he makes up some of the missed prayers until what remains is little, tarteeb returns according to some, and it is the more apparent view. And some of them say: it does not return, and that is the choice of Abu Hafs; because that which has dropped cannot be imagined to return.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage continues with additional rulings on qada. First, if one makes up missed prayers in congregation, the imam should recite loudly if those prayers are normally recited loudly (i.e., Maghrib, Isha, and Fajr). If praying alone, one has a choice between loud and silent, but loud is better, just as in the original time. Then, the passage addresses the question of whether tarteeb returns if one makes up some missed prayers until only a few remain. The stronger position (al-azhar) is that tarteeb does return. However, some scholars, including Abu Hafs, hold that it does not return because once the order is dropped, it cannot be revived. The text indicates the first view is more apparent, making it the relied-upon opinion.

Key Terms
الترتيب (al-tarteeb): The obligation to make up missed prayers in a specific order, here referring to its return after being dropped due to excessive missed prayers.
الساقط (al-saqit): That which has dropped, i.e., the obligation of tarteeb after it was dropped.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It does not return because it dropped permanently.
B) It returns according to some, and this is the more apparent view.
C) It returns only if the missed prayers are from different days.
D) It becomes optional.

Key Takeaway
When most missed prayers are made up and only a few remain, the obligation of order (tarteeb) returns according to the preferred Hanafi view.

Ruling on Dropping Order When Missed Prayers Exceed Six
States the ruling and addresses an objection about the seventh prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when the obligation of order (tartib) is dropped for missed prayers and explain the rationale based on the prevalent circumstance.

Core Idea
When missed prayers reach six and the seventh’s time enters, the obligation to pray in order is dropped.

The Hanafi school requires missed prayers to be made up in order for up to five prayers. But when the number reaches six, a concession is granted: the order is no longer obligatory. This lesson explores the precise triggering condition and the legal reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Identify the act (missed prayers) and the conditional ruling: if missed prayers exceed six, the order (tartib) is dropped.
The ruling is a rukhsah (concession) to avoid hardship.
2
Identify the precise condition
The commentator clarifies that the condition is when the missed prayers become six and the time of the seventh enters, not when the seventh is actually missed.
The matn says 'exceed six', which is explained further.
3
Analyze the objection
A logical objection is raised: at the entry of the seventh time, the missed prayers do not yet exceed six; exceeding six only occurs after the seventh time exits.
This challenges the literal reading of the condition.
4
Understand the Hanafi answer
The answer uses the principle of taghlib (predominance): in most cases, by the time the seventh time enters, the sixth has already been missed. Therefore, the ruling is based on the prevalent circumstance.
Also, the imminent possibility of missing the seventh is considered sufficient.
5
Apply the ruling
In practice, a person with six missed prayers entering the time of the seventh may pray the seventh immediately without making up the previous six first.
This constitutes a concession (rukhsah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب قضاء الفوائت إلا أن تزيد الفوائتُ على ست صلوات : فيسقط الترتيب فيها. قوله: (إلا أن تزيد الفوائتُ على ستّ صلوات: فيسقط الترتيب فيها). مراده: أن تصير الفوائت ستاً، ودخل وقت السابعة: فإنه يجوز أداء السابعة. وفيه إشكال، وهو: أن بدخول السابعة لا تزيد الفوائت على ست، وإنما ذلك بخروج وقت السابعة. والجواب: أن هذا من باب إطلاق اسم الأغلب على الكل، فإن الأغلب أن خروج السادسة لا يكون إلا بدخول السابعة، وعند دخول السابعة : يتحقق فواتُ الست والسابعة بعرضية أن تفوت. بالوتر. وقيل : معناه: إلا أن تصير الفوائتُ ستاً، وتحمل الزيادة على الست.
Chapter of Making Up Missed Prayers. Except that the missed prayers exceed six prayers: then the order (tartib) is dropped in them. His statement: (Except that the missed prayers exceed six prayers: then the order is dropped). He means: that the missed prayers become six, and the time of the seventh enters: then it is permissible to perform the seventh. There is an objection, which is: at the entry of the seventh, the missed prayers do not exceed six; rather that is at the exit of the time of the seventh. The answer: that this is from the category of naming the whole by the majority, for the majority is that the exit of the sixth does not occur except with the entry of the seventh, and at the entry of the seventh, the loss of the six and the seventh is realized by the possibility that the seventh will be lost. By the witr. And it is said: its meaning is: except that the missed prayers become six, and the increase beyond six is borne.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with explanation from the commentary on al-Quduri's Mukhtasar. It states the general rule: if the number of missed prayers reaches six or more, the obligation to perform them in order (tartib) is dropped. The commentator clarifies that this applies when the missed prayers are six and the time for the seventh enters; at that point, one may pray the seventh without waiting for the previous six. There is an objection: entering the time of the seventh does not mean the seventh is actually missed yet, only that it is about to be. The answer relies on a linguistic principle: the majority of cases have the sixth prayer expired by the time the seventh time enters, so the ruling is based on the prevalent situation. Also, the possibility (ʿurūḍīya) of the seventh becoming missed is enough to consider the total missed prayers as exceeding six. A secondary opinion says the meaning is simply that the missed prayers become six, and the 'increase' is interpreted as the additional missed prayer beyond six (i.e., the seventh) that will definitely be missed once its time exits. This shows the Hanafi precision in defining the threshold for dropping tartib.

Key Terms
الترتيب (al-tartib): The obligation to perform missed prayers in the order they were missed (e.g., Fajr before Zuhr).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when exactly is the obligation of order (tartib) dropped for missed prayers?
A) When the missed prayers reach six in number.
B) When the missed prayers are six and the time of the seventh has entered.
C) When the missed prayers exceed six, i.e., seven or more.
D) Only when the seventh prayer is actually missed after its time exits.

Key Takeaway
The obligation of order (tartib) for missed prayers is dropped when the missed prayers reach six and the time for the seventh prayer enters, based on the principle of taghlib (predominance).

Recitation in Qada' Prayers: Congregation vs. Alone
Ruling on recitation in qada' prayers in congregation vs. alone

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on recitation (jahr vs. ikhfa) when making up missed prayers in congregation and alone, and apply it to any Jahri or Sirri prayer.

Core Idea
In qada', the ruling on aloud/silent recitation mirrors the original prayer's ruling.

This short passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayirah clarifies a common scenario: when a person is making up missed prayers (qada'), does the rule on aloud and silent recitation change? The answer is simple: the same rules apply as if the prayer were performed in its time. However, there is a nuance regarding reciting alone, where the worshipper has a choice but aloud remains better.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its context
Determine whether the qada' prayer is performed in congregation (imam leading) or alone. The ruling differs.
2
Classify the prayer type
Identify if the missed prayer is from the Jahri (aloud) category: Fajr, Maghrib, 'Isha. For Sirri (Dhuhr, 'Asr), the ruling is the opposite.
Jahri prayers require aloud recitation in congregation; Sirri require silent.
3
Apply the ruling for congregation
If the imam leads a group in making up a missed Jahri prayer, he must recite aloud.
This is a wajib upon the imam.
4
Apply the ruling for solitary prayer
If praying alone, the worshipper has a choice (takhyir) between aloud and silent, but aloud is preferable (afdal).
The preference is due to aligning with the sunnah of the original time.
5
Compare with the original time (ada')
The ruling is identical to that of the prayer performed in its prescribed time. This is based on istihsan (juristic preference) that qada' follows ada' in recitation rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومتى قضى الفوائت : إن قضاها بجماعة، وكانت يُجهر فيها: جهر الإمام فيها بالقراءة، وإن قضاها وحده يتخيَّر، والجهر أفضل، كما في الوقت.
And when one makes up the missed prayers: if he makes them up in congregation, and they are prayers in which recitation is aloud, the imam recites aloud therein; and if he makes them up alone, he has a choice, and aloud is better, just as in the prescribed time.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This is a straightforward ruling from the commentary. When a person makes up missed prayers (qada'), the rules of recitation follow the rules of the prayers performed in their prescribed time (ada'). Specifically: if the missed prayer is one where recitation is normally aloud (i.e., Fajr, Maghrib, 'Isha), and the person is an imam in congregation, he must recite aloud. If he prays alone, he has a choice (takhyir) between aloud and silent recitation, but aloud is preferable (afdal) because it aligns with the original sunnah. This ruling is consistent across the Hanafi school; no dispute is mentioned here.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada'): Making up a missed obligatory prayer after its time has expired.
جهر (jahr): Reciting aloud in prayer such that others can hear.
إخفاء (ikhfa'): Reciting silently such that only the person can hear himself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He must recite silently.
B) He must recite aloud.
C) He has a choice, and aloud is better.
D) He must recite in a moderate voice.

Key Takeaway
In making up missed prayers, the ruling on recitation (jahr or ikhfa') is exactly the same as if the prayer were performed in its original time.

The Return of Tartib After Most Missed Prayers Are Made Up
Resolves a dispute about the return of tartib when only a few missed prayers remain

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two positions within the Hanafi school on whether the obligation of tartib returns when only a few missed prayers remain, explain the reconciliation offered in the text, and identify the fatwa position.

Core Idea
Tartib can return if the remaining missed prayers were made up in order; otherwise it does not.

You know that if you miss more than five prayers, the obligation to pray them in order (tartib) drops. But what if you then make up most of them and only a few remain? Does tartib return? This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on precisely this point. The commentator offers a reconciliation that turns on whether the missed prayers were made up in order or not.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on tartib
Recall that in Hanafi fiqh, tartib is wajib for up to five missed prayers. For more than five, tartib drops. This lesson considers what happens when tartib has dropped and then most are made up.
2
Identify the two views on return of tartib
Some scholars say tartib returns when only a few missed prayers remain (al-azhar). Others, like Abu Hafs, say it does not return because something that has fallen cannot return.
Both views are within the Hanafi school.
3
Understand the reconciliation
The commentator reconciles by distinguishing: if the person made up the missed prayers in order, then tartib returns; if not, it does not. This links the return of tartib to the manner of making up.
4
Examine the concrete case
The text gives a specific example: someone missed a month of prayers, made them up except one or two, then wishes to pray the on-time prayer while mindful of the missed ones. Abu Ja'far says it is not permissible (tartib must be observed), while Abu Hafs al-Kabir says it is permissible. The fatwa follows Abu Hafs al-Kabir.
This shows the practical result of the reconciliation.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if the missed prayers were made up in order, the person must pray the on-time prayer after completing them; otherwise, he may pray the on-time prayer immediately (according to the fatwa view).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قضى بعض الفوائت حتى قل ما بقي: عاد الترتيب عند البعض، وهو الأظهر. وقال بعضهم : لا يعود، وهو اختيار أبي حفص؛ لأن الساقط لا يتصور عوده. قال صاحب «الحواشي»: وهو الأصح. والتوفيق بينهما : أنه إذا قضاها مرتباً : عاد الترتيب، وإن لم يقضها مرتباً: لم يعد. بيانه: إذا ترك صلاة شهر، وقضاها، إلا صلاةً أو صلاتين، ثم صلى وقتية وهو ذاكر للباقي : قال بعضهم : لا يجوز، وإليه مال أبو جعفر. وقال بعضهم: يجوز، وإليه مال أبو حفص الكبير، وعليه الفتوى. وفي الهداية» : عود الترتيب : هو الأظهر.
And if he makes up some of the missed prayers until little remains, the order (tartib) returns according to some, and it is the most manifest. And some said: it does not return, and it is the choice of Abu Hafs; because what has fallen cannot be imagined to return. The author of 'al-Hawashi' said: it is the most correct. The reconciliation between them: if he makes them up in order, the order returns; if he does not make them up in order, it does not return. Its explanation: if he left the prayers of a month, and made them up, except one or two prayers, then he prays an on-time prayer while he is mindful of the remaining: some said: it is not permissible, and Abu Ja'far inclined to that. And some said: it is permissible, and Abu Hafs al-Kabir inclined to that, and the fatwa is upon it. And in al-Hidayah: the return of order is the most manifest.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether the obligation of order (tartib) returns when a person has made up most of his missed prayers and only a few remain. There are two positions: (1) tartib returns (al-azhar, according to some), (2) tartib does not return (choice of Abu Hafs, because something dropped cannot be reinstated). The commentator offers a reconciliation: if the person made up the missed prayers in order, then tartib returns; if not, it does not. He then gives an example: someone missed a month of prayers, made them up except one or two, then prays an on-time prayer while mindful of the remaining missed prayers. Abu Ja'far says it is not permissible (i.e., he must pray the missed prayers first), but Abu Hafs al-Kabir says it is permissible, and this is the fatwa. Al-Hidayah states that the return of order is the most manifest view. The passage shows the nuance of Hanafi methodology: the seemingly contradictory positions are reconciled by distinguishing whether the remaining missed prayers were made up in order or not.

Key Terms
الترتيب (al-tartib): The obligation to perform missed prayers in the order they were missed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) It returns only if the on-time prayer is not prayed.

Key Takeaway
The dispute on the return of tartib is resolved by distinguishing whether the remaining missed prayers were made up in order or not; the fatwa allows praying the on-time prayer first if the missed ones were not made up in order.

Completing 'Asr After Sunset with Missed Prayers: Istihsan vs. Qiyas
Resolves a dispute between al-Sarakhsi and 'Isa on completing 'Asr after sunset

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the reasoning behind istihsan and qiyas in this case and state the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
Completing the 'Asr prayer is preferred by istihsan to avoid rendering the entire prayer out of time.

When you start 'Asr in its time but sunset arrives while you owe missed prayers, the clock seems to reset. Al-Sarakhsi and 'Isa ibn Aban offer opposing views: one follows qiyas (analogy) to cut the prayer, the other uses istihsan (juristic preference) to complete it. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning behind each position, revealing the Hanafi method of reconciling conflicting proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
State the facts: a person performs part of 'Asr within its time, the sun sets, and he owes one or two missed prayers (before 'Asr) that he is mindful of.
The missed prayers were previously due before 'Asr, but the obligation of order (tartib) had been dropped due to time constraint (dhiq).
2
State the Two Positions
Al-Sarakhsi and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani say complete the 'Asr (istihsan). 'Isa ibn Aban says cut the 'Asr after sunset and begin the missed prayer (qiyas).
These are both within the Hanafi school; 'Isa is a later Hanafi scholar.
3
Analyze the Qiyas Argument
Examine 'Isa's analogy: just as a mutayammim finding water or a naked person finding clothing must stop and start over, so too the 'Asr prayer should be cut because the condition (time for 'Asr) has ended and a new obligation (missed prayers) has revived.
The effective cause (illa) is the change of circumstances that makes the original act invalid.
4
Analyze the Istihsan Argument
Imam Muhammad argues that cutting would mean the entire 'Asr is performed outside its time (the part in time would also be nullified). Completing it allows the part in time to count as ada' and the rest as qada'.
Istihsan overrides qiyas to avoid a more harmful outcome (rendering the whole prayer out of time).
5
Identify the Mufta Bihi
The relied-upon position (fatwa) is the istihsan view of al-Sarakhsi and Muhammad, making completion the operative ruling.
This is consistent with the Hanafi principle that istihsan can override qiyas when the latter leads to hardship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أدى بعض العصر في الوقت ثم غربت الشمس، وعليه صلاة أو صلاتان، قبلها وهو ذاكر لها قال السرخسي: يتمها. وطعن عيسى بن أبان في هذا، وقال الصحيح أنه يقطعها بعد الغروب، ثم يبدأ بالفائتة ; لأن الوقت قابل للقضاء، والمسقط للترتيب من الضيق قد انعدم بالغروب، وصار الوقت واسعاً; لأن المعترض في خلال الصلاة: كالموجود عند افتتاحها كالمتيمم إذا وجد الماء، والعاري إذا وجد الثوب. وما ذكره عيسى: هو القياس، لكن محمداً استحسن، فقال: لو قطعِ بعد الغروب: كان مؤدياً جميع العصر في غير وقتها، ولو أتمها: كان مؤدياً ...
And if he performed part of 'Asr in its time, then the sun set, and he owes one or two prayers before it while being mindful of them: al-Sarakhsi said: he completes it. And 'Isa ibn Aban criticized this, and said: the correct view is that he cuts it after sunset, then begins with the missed prayer; because the time is suitable for making up, and the factor that dropped the order due to constraint has ceased with sunset, and the time has become expansive; because what intervenes during the prayer is like what existed at its beginning, like the mutayammim if he finds water, and the naked person if he finds clothing. And what 'Isa mentioned is the analogy (qiyas), but Muhammad (al-Shaybani) used istihsan, and said: if he cuts after sunset, he would have performed the entire 'Asr outside its time, and if he completes it, he would have performed...
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a person who has started praying 'Asr within its time, but before completing it, the sun sets. Moreover, he owes one or two missed prayers that must be performed before 'Asr in order (tartib) had it not been dropped. Two opinions: (1) Al-Sarakhsi says he should complete the 'Asr prayer (istihsan). (2) 'Isa ibn Aban says he should cut the prayer after sunset and begin the missed prayer (qiyas), because after sunset the time constraint is removed (the missed prayers are no longer more than six?), and the existing order obligation revives (since the factor that dropped it—time constraint—is gone). 'Isa draws an analogy: if a person performing dry ablution (tayammum) finds water during prayer, or a naked person finds clothing, they must stop and start over. However, Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani adopts istihsan, reasoning that cutting the prayer would mean offering the whole 'Asr outside its time (since part was in time and part after sunset, if cut, all of it is considered outside time), whereas completing it would be considered a valid ada' (on-time) for the part done in time and qada' for the rest? The text cuts off, but the argument is clear: Muhammad's istihsan prefers completeness and avoids rendering the entire prayer out of time. This shows the Hanafi preference for istihsan over qiyas in cases involving conflicting considerations.

Key Terms
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Preferring a weaker analogy (qiyas) for a stronger reason, such as avoiding harm or following a precedent.
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogizing a new case with an existing ruling based on a common effective cause (illa).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani choose istihsan over qiyas in the case of completing 'Asr after sunset?
A) Because the missed prayers are more important.
B) Because cutting the prayer would render the entire 'Asr as performed outside its time, which is worse.
C) Because the sunset is a minor interruption.
D) Because the person did not intend to miss the prayer.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, istihsan can override qiyas when qiyas leads to a more harmful outcome; here, completing 'Asr avoids rendering the entire prayer out of time.

Return of Tartib When Making Up Missed Prayers
Intra-Hanafi dispute on return of tartib

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two Hanafi views on whether tartib returns when making up missed prayers in order, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Once lost, tartib can return if missed prayers are made up in order — the cautious opinion prevails.

The obligation of order (tartib) between prayers is a well-known Hanafi ruling. But what happens when you have many missed prayers and begin to make them up? Does tartib automatically return, or must it be actively restored? This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Ja'far and Abu Hafs al-Kabir, with the latter's view becoming the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the general rule: tartib is wajib between prayers of the same day, and between missed and timely prayers if the missed prayers are few (up to five). However, when many prayers are missed, tartib may be suspended due to necessity.
The suspension is a concession, not an abolition.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi variance
Two views exist: (1) Abu Ja'far holds that once tartib is lost, it does not return; (2) Abu Hafs al-Kabir holds that tartib returns if the missed prayers are made up in order.
Both are Hanafi scholars; this is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
3
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
Consider a scenario: a person missed a month of prayers, made up all but one or two, then performs a timely prayer while remembering the remaining missed prayers. According to Abu Ja'far, the timely prayer is invalid. According to Abu Hafs, if he made up the missed prayers in order, tartib returns and the timely prayer is valid.
The practical consequence depends on whether the missed prayers were made up in order.
4
Determine the tarjih
The author of al-Hawashi states that the view of Abu Hafs al-Kabir is the most correct (al-asahh) and that the fatwa is upon it. Al-Hidayah also affirms that the return of order is the most apparent (al-azhar).
The mufta bihi is the view that tartib can return if missed prayers are made up in order.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال صاحب «الحواشي»: وهو الأصح.
والتوفيق بينهما : أنه إذا قضاها مرتباً : عاد الترتيب، وإن لم يقضها مرتباً: لم يعد.
بيانه: إذا ترك صلاة شهر، وقضاها، إلا صلاةً أو صلاتين، ثم صلى وقتية وهو ذاكر للباقي : قال بعضهم : لا يجوز، وإليه مال أبو جعفر. وقال بعضهم: يجوز، وإليه مال أبو حفص الكبير، وعليه الفتوى. وفي الهداية» (۱) : عود الترتيب : هو الأظهر.
The author of al-Hawashi said: And it is the most correct. And the reconciliation between them is that if he makes them up in order, the order returns, and if he does not make them up in order, it does not return. Its explanation: If he left a month of prayers, and made them up except for one or two prayers, then he performed a timely prayer while remembering the remaining ones, some said: it is not permissible, and Abu Ja'far inclined towards this. And some said: it is permissible, and Abu Hafs al-Kabir inclined towards this, and the fatwa is upon it. And in al-Hidayah: the return of order is the most apparent.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on the return of the order (tartib) between missed prayers and timely prayers after one has begun making up missed prayers. The author of al-Hawashi presents two views among Hanafi scholars. The first view, held by Abu Ja'far (al-Tahawi), is that once tartib has been lost due to forgetfulness or compulsion, it does not return; thus it is invalid to perform a timely prayer while still owing missed prayers. The second view, held by Abu Hafs al-Kabir (the author of al-Hidayah), is that if one makes up the missed prayers in the correct chronological order, tartib returns, making it obligatory to observe the order. However, if one does not make them up in order, tartib remains suspended. The relied-upon view for fatwa is that of Abu Hafs al-Kabir, as stated in al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
ترتيب (tartib): The prescribed order between prayers, such that a timely prayer must not precede a missed prayer when there is time to make it up.
قضاء الفوائت (qada' al-fawa'it): Making up prayers that were missed due to sleep, forgetfulness, or other valid excuses.
أبو جعفر (Abu Ja'far): A Hanafi scholar (al-Tahawi) who held that tartib does not return once lost.
أبو حفص الكبير (Abu Hafs al-Kabir): A Hanafi scholar (Ahmad ibn Hafs al-Bukhari) who held that tartib returns if missed prayers are made up in order.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person missed a month of prayers and then made up all except two, and then performed a timely prayer while remembering the two missed prayers, what is the relied-upon Hanafi ruling?
A) The timely prayer is invalid because tartib did not return.
B) The timely prayer is valid because tartib returns if missed prayers are made up in order.
C) The timely prayer is invalid regardless of making up in order.
D) The person must repeat the timely prayer after making up all missed prayers.

Key Takeaway
Tartib can return if missed prayers are made up in order; this is the mufta bihi view in the Hanafi school.

Completing 'Asr After Sunset When Owing Missed Prayers
Ruling on completing 'Asr prayer in time followed by sunset with missed prayers owed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on completing 'Asr when sunset occurs with missed prayers owed, and explain the istihsan reasoning that overrides qiyas.

Core Idea
If completing 'Asr after sunset keeps it in its time, istihsan allows it even if tartib is violated.

This passage presents a classic Hanafi debate between qiyas and istihsan. When a person starts 'Asr in its time but sunset occurs while he owes missed prayers, should he break the prayer? The qiyas says yes, but Imam Muhammad's istihsan says complete it to preserve its time-based validity. Understanding this ruling illustrates the school's pragmatic balance between strict analogy and equitable flexibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Problem
State the scenario: a person begins 'Asr within its time, the sun sets before he finishes, and he owes one or two missed prayers before 'Asr (e.g., Dhuhr) and remembers them.
The core issue: does the obligation of tartib (order) require breaking the current prayer to make up the missed one first?
2
State the Qiyas Position
Isa ibn Aban argues that the person should break the 'Asr after sunset and start with the missed prayer. His reasoning: the narrowness of time that previously excused tartib is gone, so tartib returns. He analogizes to the mutayammim who finds water during prayer—he must break.
Qiyas here compares the removal of an excuse (narrowness) to finding water during tayammum.
3
State the Istihsan Position
Imam Muhammad (al-Shaybani) argues that the person should complete the 'Asr prayer. His reasoning: if he breaks it, the entire 'Asr will be performed outside its time, which is a greater loss. Completing it ensures the prayer is within its valid time. Moreover, once tartib is dropped due to narrowness, it does not return for that same prayer.
Istihsan overrides qiyas based on the principle of preventing greater harm.
4
Analyze the Ila
The effective cause (illa) in the qiyas is the removal of the excuse (narrowness of time). However, Imam Muhammad identifies a stronger consideration: the preservation of the act in its proper time outweighs the reintroduction of tartib for that prayer.
This is a classic instance of tahqiq al-manat: the illa is not applied rigidly because its application leads to a worse outcome.
5
Apply the Hanafi Tarjih
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is Imam Muhammad's istihsan: complete the 'Asr prayer, then make up the missed prayer afterwards. This is the view of al-Sarakhsi and is consistent with the Hanafi preference for istihsan over qiyas when maslahah dictates.
The tarjih authority here is the later Hanafi masters who followed Imam Muhammad's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أدى بعض العصر في الوقت ثم غربت الشمس، وعليه صلاة أو صلاتان ،قبلها وهو ذاكر لها قال السرخسي: يتمها.
وطعن عيسى بن أبان في هذا، وقال الصحيح أنه يقطعها بعد الغروب، ثم يبدأ بالفائتة ; لأن الوقت قابل للقضاء، والمسقط للترتيب من الضيق قد انعدم بالغروب، وصار الوقت واسعاً; لأن المعترض في خلال الصلاة: كالموجود عند افتتاحها كالمتيمم إذا وجد الماء، والعاري إذا وجد الثوب.
وما ذكره عيسى: هو القياس، لكن محمداً استحسن، فقال: لو قطعِ بعد الغروب: كان مؤدياً جميع العصر في غير وقتها، ولو أتمها: كان مؤدياً لها في وقتها، فكان أولى.
ولأنه عند الضيق قد سقط عنه الترتيب في هذه الصلاة، ومتى سقط في صلاة: لا يعود في تلك الصلاة.
بخلاف النسيان، فهناك الترتيب غير ساقط، لكن يُعذر للجهل، فإذا زال العذر قبل الفراغ من الصلاة: بقي عليه مراعاة الترتيب كما كان; لأنه لما زال العُذر في خلال الصلاة: صار كأن لم يكن.
And if he performed part of 'Asr within the time, then the sun set, and upon him is a prayer or two prayers before it, and he remembers them, al-Sarakhsi said: He completes it. Isa ibn Aban objected to this and said the correct view is that he cuts it after sunset, then begins with the missed prayer, because the time is still valid for making up, and the remover of tartib due to narrowness has ceased with sunset, and the time becomes wide; because what intervenes during the prayer is like what existed at its beginning, like the mutayammim when he finds water, and the unclothed when he finds clothing. What Isa mentioned is analogical reasoning (qiyas), but Muhammad (al-Shaybani) used istihsan, saying: If he cuts after sunset, he would be performing all of 'Asr outside its time, but if he completes it, he would be performing it in its time, so it is better. And because during narrowness, tartib has fallen from this prayer, and whenever it falls in a prayer, it does not return in that prayer. Unlike forgetfulness, for there tartib is not fallen, but he is excused due to ignorance, so when the excuse ceases before finishing the prayer, he must observe tartib as before; because when the excuse ceases during the prayer, it becomes as if it never existed.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific scenario: a person begins 'Asr prayer within its time, but before completing it, the sun sets. Meanwhile, the person owes one or two missed prayers before 'Asr and remembers them. The question is whether he should complete the 'Asr prayer or break it to make up the missed prayers first? Al-Sarakhsi says he should complete the 'Asr prayer. Isa ibn Aban disagrees, arguing that after sunset the obligation of tartib is reinstated because the time constraint that previously excused tartib no longer exists; thus he should break the 'Asr and make up the missed prayers. Isa supports his view with qiyas: like the case of a person who starts prayer with tayammum and then finds water, or one who starts prayer naked and then finds clothing – they must break the prayer. However, Imam Muhammad (al-Shaybani) uses istihsan and says it is better to complete the 'Asr prayer because if he breaks it, the entire 'Asr will be performed outside its time, whereas completing it ensures it is performed in its time. Moreover, when tartib was dropped due to the narrowness of time (i.e., the fear of missing the 'Asr time), it applies to that entire prayer; once dropped, it does not return within that same prayer. This is different from forgetfulness, where tartib is not dropped but merely excused; if the excuse ceases during the prayer, tartib must be observed.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): A juristic preference that deviates from strict qiyas in favor of a more equitable outcome.
ترتيب (tartib): Order between prayers; here specifically the obligation to pray missed prayers before timely ones.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad's istihsan, what should a person do if he started 'Asr within time, but the sun set while he owes a missed prayer before 'Asr and remembers it?
A) Break the 'Asr and make up the missed prayer immediately.
B) Complete the 'Asr prayer without making up the missed prayer at all.
C) Complete the 'Asr prayer and then make up the missed prayer after.
D) Repeat both prayers after 'Asr time.

Key Takeaway
When completing a prayer keeps it within its time, Hanafi istihsan allows overriding the order of prayers.

Making Up an Unknown Missed Prayer and the Witr–Fajr Conflict
Ruling on making up an unknown missed prayer and related Witr issue.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for making up an unknown missed prayer and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement on the validity of Fajr when Witr is missed.

Core Idea
Uncertainty demands precaution: repeat all five prayers unless a strong guess exists, but missing Witr may invalidate a subsequent Fajr.

This lesson addresses two common scenarios of doubt in salah: when a person knows one prayer of the day is missed but cannot identify it, and when someone prays Fajr while remembering Witr is still owed. The Hanafi school provides clear rules based on precaution (ihtiyat) and the principle of tartib (order). We will also compare the Hanafi view with those of other scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The passage begins by stating the Hanafi default: repeat all five prayers of a day and night as precaution (ihtiyat) when the specific missed prayer is unknown.
This applies only if the person has no predominant opinion (ghalib al-ra'y).
2
Understand the role of predominant opinion
If the person has a strong guess about which prayer was missed, he acts on it and only makes up that prayer.
This flexibility shows that ihtiyat is a secondary measure.
3
Compare with other schools
The text presents the views of Al-Thawri (pray Maghrib, Fajr, then a four-rak'ah prayer for the remaining three) and Bishr al-Marisi (single four-rak'ah prayer with multiple intentions). Contrast their methods with the Hanafi position.
The Hanafi response emphasizes thoroughness.
4
Analyze the Witr–Fajr issue
The passage then shifts: if someone prays Fajr while remembering he has not prayed Witr, Abu Hanifa rules Fajr is invalid (except at the very end of its time), while the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) rule it is valid.
This intra-Hanafi dispute hinges on the requirement of tartib (order) between witr and fajr.
5
Note the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: under Abu Hanifa, the Fajr must be repeated; under the Sahibayn, it stands and only Witr needs to be made up.
The mufta bihi (operative view) is that of the Sahibayn, as it is the more lenient and widely adopted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو فاتته صلاة من يوم وليلة لا يدري أي صلاة هي: فإنه يعيد صلاة يوم وليلة ; احتياطاً إذا لم يكن له رأي، فإن كان له رأي: عمل على غالب رأيه. وقال الثوري: يصلي المغرب والفجر، ثم يصلي أربع ركعات ينوي بها الظهر والعصر والعشاء; لأن هذه الصلوات الثلاث عددها متفق عليه. وقال بشر المريسي يصلي أربع ركعات، يقعد في الثانية والثالثة والرابعة ينوي بها ما عليه ; لأنها إن كانت الفجر: فقد أدى ركعتين، وخرج منها إلى صلاة أخرى بانتقاله وكذا في المغرب وبقية الصلوات. ولو صلى الفجر وهو ذاكر أنه لم يوتر: فصلاة الفجر فاسدة عند أبي حنيفة، إلا أن يكون صلى الفجر في آخر وقتها. وعندهما: صلاة الفجر تامة.
And if a prayer of a day and night is missed by him, and he does not know which prayer it is: then he repeats the prayers of a day and night, as a precaution, if he does not have an opinion. If he has an opinion, he acts upon his predominant opinion. Al-Thawri said: He prays Maghrib and Fajr, then prays four rak'ahs intending with them Dhuhr, 'Asr, and 'Isha; because the number of these three prayers is agreed upon. Bishr al-Marisi said: He prays four rak'ahs, sitting in the second, third, and fourth, intending with them what is upon him; because if it was Fajr, he has performed two rak'ahs and exited to another prayer by his transition, and similarly for Maghrib and the rest of the prayers. And if he prays Fajr while remembering that he has not performed Witr, then the Fajr prayer is invalid according to Abu Hanifah, unless he prayed Fajr at its very end. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the Fajr prayer is complete.
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage covers two distinct but related issues: first, how to make up a missed prayer when one is uncertain which specific prayer of the day was missed. The Hanafi position is to repeat all five prayers of a day and night as a precaution if one has no predominant guess. If one has a guess, he acts on it. Al-Thawri suggests a method focusing on the prayers whose rak'at counts differ, while Bishr al-Marisi proposes a single four-rak'ah prayer with multiple intentions. The relied-upon Hanafi view is the precautionary repetition of all five. The second issue (included in the same text) is the case of praying Fajr while remembering that one has not yet prayed Witr. According to Abu Hanifa, the Fajr is invalid because Witr is wajib and its omission violates tartib, unless the Fajr was prayed at the very end of its time when tartib is no longer required. The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the Fajr prayer is valid because the missed Witr does not invalidate it.

Key Terms
احتياط (ihtiyat): Precautionary measure to ensure fulfillment of religious obligation.
غالب الرأي (ghalib al-ra'y): Predominant opinion or strong guess about which prayer was missed.
وتر (witr): Odd-numbered prayer performed after Isha, considered wajib in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the default action for a person who missed one prayer of a day and night but does not know which one?
A) Pray only the five prayers separately.
B) Pray a single four-rak'ah prayer with multiple intentions.
C) Repeat all five prayers of a day and night as a precaution.
D) Pray only Maghrib and Fajr, then a four-rak'ah prayer.

Key Takeaway
In cases of uncertainty about which prayer was missed, the Hanafi school prioritizes precaution by repeating all five, but allows acting on a predominant opinion when available; on the separate issue of Witr and Fajr, the mufta bihi is that Fajr remains valid despite missing Witr.
